Explanation of the meaning of religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (3) A divine ordination (1), driving (2) those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice (4), to what is good for them essentially (5). That is: rulings placed by God (6) Almighty for the servants, impelling to essential good, which is... (1) His saying: 'divine' means: these placed rulings are attributed to the divine, and He is God, and it is a differentia excluding human ordinations, i.e., those by their acquisition, such as political laws to which the governance of the world is referred, like the science of household management, and industrial ordinations like carpentry; these are not called religion, and like the books that the sages used to compose in ancient times on the governance of subjects and the reform of cities, by which kings of those who have no revealed law rule. For although the Creator of all things is God, humans have acquisition in these. If you say: then it would follow that ijtihādī rulings of fiqh are not part of religion because humans have acquisition in them, and only what is transmitted as a text with no dispute is from it. The answer: they are certainly from religion, and they are a divine subject; the only matter is that it is hidden from us, and the mujtahid undertakes to manifest them and deduce them by the rules of the Sharia, and he has no role in placing them. Al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: 'driving' means: impelling and motivating; because the responsible person, when he hears what results from performing the obligatory deed of reward, is driven to do it, or hears what he obtains from performing the forbidden deed of punishment, is driven to leave it. It is a second differentia excluding divine ordinations that are not driving, like the growth of the earth and the raining of the sky. Thus some people have exemplified. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'by their choice' excludes the coercive, like the pain that drives to moaning unwillingly. It contains that guidance is not necessary from this ordination, for this choice may be withheld from whom God wills to lead astray. Al-Amīr. (4) His saying: 'the praiseworthy' is in the accusative as an operative case of 'choice', meaning: He drives them due to their choice of the praiseworthy matter, which is Islam; and in the genitive it is an adjective for choice. By it the blameworthy is excluded, like the ability for disbelief; for it is a divine ordination driving those with intellects by their blameworthy choice, and like love of this world, for it is a divine ordination that drives those with intellects to abandon zakāt by their blameworthy choice. And whenever choice is praiseworthy, it only drives to good. So His saying: 'to what is good for them' He mentioned it as a means to His saying: 'essentially'. Al-Shanwānī. (5) His saying: 'essentially' and essentially good is eternal happiness; the divine ordinations driving for mere worldly benefit are excluded, like the faculties of crafts created in humans. Al-Amīr. (6) His saying: 'God placed it' know that the legal ruling, as previously mentioned, is applied to the complete relations that came = Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (†) * Eternal happiness (1), and at the end of this topic (2) comes its division into general and special. = • So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance to the truth. (Fā) When the mentioned Prophet was sent, he guided the creation, i.e., all the two worlds, himself... from the Sharia, like the affirmation of obligation for prayer and zakāt, and it is applied to the attributes of taklīf which are: obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permission. If this second meaning is intended, then difficulty arises in His saying: 'placed it', because placement is creation, so it indicates that they are originated, while the mentioned attributes are eternal, because obligation, for example, is an expression of God's eternal speech, indicating the request of action with determination. And it is answered: that the actual relation is originated when the responsible person exists, if it is taken into their concept, then they are originated. So His saying: 'God placed it' means in consideration of the actual relation, originated. And if the first meaning is intended, then no difficulty. The apparent meaning. And the meaning of 'God placed it' is: He renewed and established them after non-existence, and do not say: He brought them into existence; because this is what we mean by relations, and relations are mental considerations that have no existence. Al-Shanwānī. (1) His saying: 'and it is happiness' the tadhkīr and ta'nīth of this pronoun are permissible with regard to the referent and 'good'. And indeed essentially good is happiness because it is intended essentially and originally, and nothing else reaches it in greatness. And eternal happiness is Paradise. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying: 'and at the end of the topic' i.e., in his saying: 'And He specially honored the best of creation that He has indeed completed with him all, our Lord, and generalized, so understand that his religion is general, and the religion of others is particular.' The intended meaning of 'end of the topic' is what corresponds to the beginning. And it is better that what is intended by its end is the author's saying: 'And preserve religion, then life, then property, lineage.' For the commentator said there: And it is what God has legislated for His servants of rulings, general like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad, or particular like the Sharia of Jesus. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'its division' i.e., of religion, his saying: 'into general' like his Sharia. And his saying: 'and special' like the Sharia of Jesus. And it is said: the general is the science of tawhid, and the special is the science of fiqh that pertains to it. The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawī said: they are signs of its existence. Some organized them: The matters of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving what is forbidden, and soundness of contract. Truthfulness of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity. Fulfillment of covenant is performing the obligations. Leaving what is forbidden is avoiding the prohibitions. Soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Shanwānī.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part explanation of the meaning of religion (ad-din) and its relation to tawhid before the Prophet's mission. The author begins by defining religion as 'a divine ordination driving those with intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, to what is essentially good for them.' Each term in this definition is then expanded through footnotes. The first footnote clarifies that 'divine' (ilāhi) distinguishes religion from human-made laws, including political systems and crafts—though humans may acquire these, rulings of ijtihad are still divine in origin, only hidden. The second footnote explains 'driving' (sa'iq) as impelling through reward and punishment, excluding natural phenomena like rain. The third footnote specifies that choice is volitional, not coercive, and does not guarantee guidance. The fourth footnote distinguishes 'praiseworthy choice' as Islam, excluding blameworthy choices like disbelief. The fifth footnote identifies 'essentially good' as eternal happiness (sa'adat al-ākhira). The sixth footnote addresses a theological nuance: legal rulings as attributes (like obligation) are eternal, but their actual relation to created beings is originated—'place' (waḍ') refers to this originated relation. After this, the passage transitions to the Prophet's mission: 'So he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword and his guidance.' A commentary explains that the Prophet guided both humans and jinn, and addresses an objection about the word 'place' (waḍa'a) implying creation of rulings, which would conflict with the eternity of divine attributes. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the created relation. Finally, the passage discusses the division of religion into general (the Sharia of Muhammad) and specific (previous Sharias), and enumerates four pillars of religion: truthfulness of intention, fulfillment of covenant, leaving the forbidden, and soundness of belief.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet's religion is only for Arabs or that previous Sharias are still valid.

According to the passage, what are the four matters of religion?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet's Sharia is universal, have you truly internalized that every action of yours—whether intention, obligation, avoidance of sin, or belief—is part of this 'general religion'? Where in your daily life do you treat Islam as merely a private or cultural matter rather than a universal standard for all of humanity?
?
Al-Nawawi lists 'truthfulness of intention' as a sign of religion. When you perform an act of worship, do you examine whether your intention is genuinely for Allah alone, or do you allow even a sliver of seeking praise from others? How will you check your intention in your next prayer or charity?
?
The passage mentions that previous Sharias were specific but yours is general. Does this awareness make you more grateful for the completeness of Muhammad's message, or does it make you complacent? What specific obligation are you neglecting that you need to fulfill today to live out this universal Sharia?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Exclusive Meaning of al-Ḥaqq for Allah
Commentator defines 'al-Ḥaqq' as Allah's self-subsistent existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'al-Ḥaqq' is reserved exclusively for Allah in this theological context.

Core Idea
Al-Ḥaqq is not just truth—it is Allah’s self-subsistent existence.

This lesson unpacks a key term from the poem: 'dīn al-ḥaqq'. The commentator explains that 'al-Ḥaqq' here refers specifically to Allah, because only He has permanent existence by His own essence. This clarification prevents misunderstanding and establishes a foundational concept in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how the commentator defines 'al-Ḥaqq' and then gives the reason.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لذاته' (by His essence).
2
Identify the key argument
The commentator says that no one else deserves the description 'al-Ḥaqq' because existence by essence is unique to Allah. Identify the two points: existence not preceded by non-existence and not followed by non-existence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is this important? This shows that al-Ḥaqq is a proper attribute of Allah, distinguishing Him from all contingent beings.
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين، وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته. وبواسطة، ودلَّهم (لِدِين) أي : على دين (الحَقِّ) أي : المُتحقِّقِ والثَّابِتِ وجوده ، وهو الله تعالى، ولا يستحقُ هذا الوصف غيره ؛ لأنَّ وجودَهُ لذاته لا يسبقه عدم، ولا يلحقه عدم.
Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its lack of Tawhid before his mission. And by intermediary, and he guided them to the religion of al-Ḥaqq, meaning: to the religion of al-Ḥaqq, i.e., the realized and stable in His existence, and He is Allah the Exalted, and none other deserves this description; because His existence is by His essence, preceded by no non-existence and followed by no non-existence.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'dīn al-ḥaqq' as used in the poem. It explains that 'al-Ḥaqq' here refers to the One whose existence is realized and permanent, namely Allah the Exalted. The commentary then states that no other being deserves this attribute, because Allah's existence is by His own essence (li-dhātihi)—it is not preceded by non-existence nor will it be followed by non-existence. This establishes the exclusive right of Allah to the name 'al-Ḥaqq' in this sense, contrasting with contingent beings that are preceded and followed by non-existence.

Key Terms
الحَقّ (al-Ḥaqq)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
لذاته (li-dhātihi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'al-Ḥaqq' can be applied to anything true or real.
Correction: Here it is specifically the proper name of Allah, denoting His self-subsistent existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentary say that no one other than Allah deserves the description 'al-Ḥaqq'?
A) Because Allah's existence is necessary and eternal by His essence, while others are contingent and perishable.
B) Because the word 'al-Ḥaqq' means 'creator' and only Allah creates.
C) Because the word 'al-Ḥaqq' means 'worshiped' and only Allah is worshiped.
D) Because the word 'al-Ḥaqq' means 'truth' and only Allah tells the truth.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ḥaqq as a name of Allah signifies that His existence is eternal and necessary by His essence, unique to Him alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is al-Ḥaqq, the self-subsistent One, then your entire existence is dependent on Him every moment. Have you truly internalized that your next breath is not guaranteed by your own power but by His sustained will? How does that change your reliance on Him today? Will you pause right now and thank Him for keeping you in existence?
?
The passage says Allah’s existence is not followed by non-existence. Yet you know that you will die. Does your daily life reflect that your ultimate goal is meeting al-Ḥaqq, or do you act as if this world is permanent? What is one attachment you need to loosen because it distracts you from the One who truly remains?
?
You claim to believe in Allah as al-Ḥaqq, but do you live as though He is the only reality that matters? In the next 24 hours, what is one decision you can make that would prove your belief in His exclusive self-subsistence—by trusting Him alone for a need you usually rely on yourself or others to fulfill?

The Meaning of al-Ḥaqq and Its Distinction from al-Ṣidq
Commentary defines al-ḥaqq as correspondence to reality and distinguishes it from al-ṣidq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of al-ḥaqq as correspondence to reality and how it differs from al-ṣidq in scope and logical perspective.

Core Idea
Al-ḥaqq is broader than al-ṣidq; it applies to judgments, beliefs, and religions, while al-ṣidq is mostly for statements.

This commentary unpacks the term 'al-ḥaqq' used in the poem. It defines it as the correspondence of a judgment to reality, and then clarifies its application across different domains. The passage also draws a careful linguistic and conceptual distinction between al-ḥaqq and al-ṣidq, showing how each term views the relationship between judgment and reality from a different angle.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines al-ḥaqq and immediately gives examples from statements, beliefs, and religions.
Pay attention to the phrases 'muṭābaqat al-ḥukm al-wāqiʿ' – this is the core definition.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage says al-ḥaqq is applied to statements (aqwāl), beliefs (ʿaqāʾid), and religions (adyān). It then expands on 'religions' and 'schools' (madhāhib) with examples. Make sure you understand how each contains al-ḥaqq.
Note that 'madhāhib' are the judgments of mujtahids, and they also contain truth.
3
Understand the distinction with al-ṣidq
The passage explicitly contrasts al-ḥaqq with al-ṣidq. While al-ḥaqq is used uniformly across all categories, al-ṣidq is mostly confined to statements. This is a key difference.
Ask yourself: why might al-ṣidq be more restricted? The answer lies in the next step.
4
Grasp the two notional attributes
The passage ends by explaining that a judgment corresponding to reality has two attributes: one called al-ṣidq (when reality is the standard) and one called al-ḥaqq (when the judgment is the standard). This subtle point shows the technical precision of kalam terminology.
The difference in Arabic grammar (kasr vs. fatḥ on the bā') mirrors the difference in perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهَدْيِهِ لِلْحَقِّ أي: وأرشدهم بدَلالته على الحقِّ، المراد منه: مطابقة الحكم الواقع، وهو بهذا المعنى يطلق على الأقوال، والعقائد، والأديان. قوله: (والأديان) كقولك: الصلاة واجبة، فثبوت الوجوب للصلاة دين، وهذا الثبوت مشتمل على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على الصفة، وقوله: والمذاهب أي: النسب التامة التي ذهب إليها المجتهد، كقوله: الوتر مندوب، فثبوت الندب للوتر مذهب، أي: نسبة ذهب إليها المجتهد، وهي مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته، فعلمت أن العقائد والأديان والمذاهب مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته، بمعنى قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وأما اشتماله على الحق فمن اشتمال الدال على وصف المدلول. قوله: (يطلق على الأقوال والعقائد والأديان ... إلخ) بمعنى استعمال الحق في الكل على حد سواء، بخلاف الصدق فقد كثر استعماله في الأقوال خاصة. واعلم أن الحكم المطابق للواقع له صفتان اعتباریتان: كونه مطابقا بالكسر فيقال له الصدق؛ لأنه الأصل الذي يجب اعتباره ووضع الاسم بإزائه، فإن الأقرب إلى الطبع أن يجعل الواقع أصلا، ويقاس عليه الحكم الذي يتعرف حاله. وكونه مطابقا للواقع بفتح الباء ويقال له الحق بالمعنى المصدري؛ لأنه في الأصل بمعنى:
And his guidance to al-Ḥaqq, i.e., he guided them by his indication of al-Ḥaqq. What is meant by it is: the correspondence of a judgment to reality. It is in this sense applied to statements, beliefs, and religions. He said: 'and religions' like your saying: prayer is obligatory, so the establishment of obligation for prayer is a religion (dīn), and this establishment contains al-ḥaqq by way of the description's inherence in the described. And his saying: 'and schools (madhāhib)' i.e., the complete relations that the mujtahid held, like his saying: the witr is recommended, so the establishment of recommendation for witr is a school (madhhab), i.e., a relation held by the mujtahid, and it contains al-ḥaqq by way of the description's inherence in its described. So you know that beliefs, religions, and schools contain al-ḥaqq by way of the description's inherence in the described, meaning the quality's subsistence in the qualified. As for its containing al-ḥaqq, it is by way of the indicator's containing the description of the indicated. He said: 'it is applied to statements, beliefs, and religions' meaning that the use of al-ḥaqq in all is the same, unlike al-ṣidq which is frequently used especially for statements. Know that the judgment corresponding to reality has two notional attributes: its being corresponding in terms of the object (bi al-kasr) is called al-ṣidq, because it is the original that should be considered and the name placed in counterpoint; for what is closer to nature is to make reality the original and compare to it the judgment whose state is known. And its being corresponding to reality in terms of the subject (bi al-fatḥ) is called al-ḥaqq in the verbal noun sense, because it originally means:
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'his guidance to al-Ḥaqq' from the poem. It defines 'al-Ḥaqq' as 'the correspondence of a judgment to reality' (muṭābaqat al-ḥukm al-wāqiʿ). This definition applies to statements (aqwāl), beliefs (ʿaqāʾid), and religions (adyān). The commentary then elaborates on 'religions' and 'schools' (madhāhib) as examples: when we say 'prayer is obligatory', the judgment of obligation is a religious truth; when a mujtahid says 'witr is recommended', that judgment is a school. In both cases, the truth (ḥaqq) inheres in the judgment as a quality in its subject. The passage then contrasts 'al-ḥaqq' with 'al-ṣidq' (truthfulness). Whereas 'al-ḥaqq' is used uniformly for statements, beliefs, and religions, 'al-ṣidq' is mostly confined to statements. Finally, it gives a subtle linguistic distinction: a judgment that corresponds to reality can be considered in two ways—if we view it as corresponding to reality (with the particle 'bi' taking kasra on the letter bāʾ), it is called 'ṣidq' because reality is the standard; if we view reality as being corresponded to (with fatḥa on the bāʾ), it is called 'ḥaqq' in the verbal noun sense. This nuanced analysis demonstrates the precise terminology of kalam.

Key Terms
الحق (al-ḥaqq)
الصدق (al-ṣidq)
مطابقة الحكم الواقع (muṭābaqat al-ḥukm al-wāqiʿ)
المذهب (al-madhhab)
الدين (al-dīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-ḥaqq and al-ṣidq are synonyms meaning 'true' or 'truth'.
Correction: They are distinct: al-ṣidq is primarily for statements and views reality as the standard; al-ḥaqq is broader and views the judgment as the standard.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the primary difference between 'al-ḥaqq' and 'al-ṣidq'?
Al-ḥaqq is used only for beliefs, while al-ṣidq is used for statements.
Al-ḥaqq applies to statements, beliefs, and religions equally, while al-ṣidq is mostly used for statements.
Al-ḥaqq means 'creator' and al-ṣidq means 'truthful'.
There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Al-ḥaqq is the correspondence of a judgment to reality, applied broadly, while al-ṣidq is a narrower term focusing on statements with reality as the benchmark.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim something to be 'true', do you check if it corresponds to reality or if it simply matches your own desires? How often do you confuse personal comfort with objective truth?
?
The passage says that even entire religions and schools of thought contain truth (al-ḥaqq). Does your belonging to a group make you feel you already possess the full truth, or does it drive you to seek more correspondence with reality?
?
You use words like 'truth' and 'truthfulness' loosely. Today, will you pause before speaking and ask: Am I being truthful (ṣidq) with my words, and is my overall worldview aligned with reality (ḥaqq)?

The Meaning and Scope of al-Haqq
Defining al-haqq and its scope in comparison to al-sidq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-haqq applies to all statements, beliefs, religions, and schools, and how it differs from al-sidq in usage.

Core Idea
Al-haqq is in every valid judgment, while al-sidq is mainly used for statements.

The commentator begins by explaining that the term 'truth' (al-haqq) is not limited to statements but applies to all cognitive content that contains a correct judgment. He illustrates this with examples from religions and schools of jurisprudence. This lesson establishes the broad scope of al-haqq and its difference from al-sidq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples of prayer and witr.
2
Identify how haqq applies to different domains
The passage shows that haqq is contained in religions and schools. Note how the attribute inheres in the described.
3
Note the distinction from sidq
The commentator states that al-haqq is used equally in all, while al-sidq is especially used in statements. This is the key difference.
This distinction is important for later lessons.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Consider how using the correct term can affect theological precision. The distinction helps avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والأديان) كقولك : الصلاة واجبة ، فثبوت الوجوب للصلاة دين ، وهذا الثبوت مشتمل على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على الصفة، وقوله : والمذاهب أي: النسب التامة التي ذهب إليها المجتهد، كقوله : الوتر مندوب، فثبوت الندب للوتر مذهب، أي: نسبة ذهب إليها المجتهد ، وهي مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته ، فعلمت أن العقائد والأديان والمذاهب مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته ، بمعنى قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وأما اشتماله على الحق فمن اشتمال الدال على وصف المدلول . قوله: (يطلق على الأقوال والعقائد والأديان ... إلخ) بمعنى استعمال الحق في الكل على حد سواء، بخلاف الصدق فقد كثر استعماله في الأقوال خاصة. واعلم أن الحكم المطابق للواقع له صفتان اعتباریتان: كونه مطابقا بالكسر فيقال له الصدق ؛ لأنه الأصل الذي يجب اعتباره ووضع الاسم بإزائه ، فإن الأقرب إلى الطبع أن يجعل الواقع أصلا ، ويقاس عليه الحكم الذي يتعرف حاله. وكونه مطابقا للواقع بفتح الباء ويقال له الحق بالمعنى المصدري ؛ لأنه في الأصل بمعنى: التحقق ، والحكم في هذا الاعتبار جعل أصلا ثانيا حتى قيس به الواقع والصفتان متلازمتان ؛ لأن المفاعلة للمشاركة، ولا تفاوت بين الحق والصدق إلا بما تقدم، ويقال هذا كلام صدق أي: مطابق للواقع ، وهذا كلام حق أي مطابق للواقع، أي: إن ما أفاده الكلام مطابق للواقع ، فالأسهل أنهما شيء واحد وهو مطابق الخبر للواقع ، فالواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيره ولا يقاس على غيره، فتلاحظ أن غيره هل طابقه أو لا ، لا أنه هل طابق غيره أو لا وإن كانت المفاعلة من الجانبين ، ألا ترى أنك تقول : جالس الوزير السلطان ، ولا تقول: جالس السلطان الوزير الشنواني . وضده الباطل، أي: يضاده إطلاقا واستعمالا ، فهو عدم مطابقة الحكم الواقع ، فيقال: قول باطل ودين باطل ، أي : لم يطابق الواقع . فقولك : الله معدوم له نسبة كلامية ، وهي ثبوت العدم لله تعالى والنسبة الواقعية ثبوت الوجود، فالنسبة الكلامية غير مطابقة للنسبة الواقعية ، فعدم المطابقة هو الباطل. الشنواني .
His saying: (and the religions) like your saying: 'Prayer is obligatory.' So the affirmation of obligation for prayer is a religion, and this affirmation contains the truth (al-haqq) in the manner of the attribute containing the described. And his saying: 'and the schools' meaning the complete relations that the mujtahid adopted, like his saying: 'The witr is recommended.' So the affirmation of recommendation for witr is a school, i.e., a relation adopted by the mujtahid, and it contains the truth in the manner of the attribute containing its described. So you know that beliefs, religions, and schools contain the truth in the manner of the attribute containing its described, meaning the subsistence of the attribute in the described. As for its containing the truth, it is from the containing of the signifier for the description of the signified. His saying: 'It is applied to statements, beliefs, religions... etc.' meaning that the use of 'truth' in all is equal, contrary to 'truthfulness (al-sidq)' which is often used especially in statements. Know that the judgment corresponding to reality has two conceptual attributes: being a correspondence with a kasra, so it is called 'truthfulness' because it is the origin that must be considered and the name placed for it, for it is closer to nature to make reality the origin and measure the judgment against it to know its state. And being a correspondence with a fatha, so it is called 'the truth' in the verbal sense, because it originally means actualization, and the judgment in this consideration is made a second origin until reality is measured against it. The two attributes are inseparable because the mutafā‘ala is for sharing. There is no difference between truth and truthfulness except what has been mentioned. It is said 'This is truthful speech,' i.e., corresponding to reality, and 'This is true speech,' i.e., corresponding to reality, i.e., what the speech indicates is corresponding to reality. The easiest view is that they are one thing, namely the correspondence of the report to reality. For reality is something fixed in itself, measured against others and not measured by others. So you observe whether other things correspond to it or not, not whether it corresponds to others or not, even though the mutafā‘ala is from both sides. Do you not see that you say 'The vizier sat with the sultan,' and do not say 'The sultan sat with the vizier'? And its opposite is falsehood (al-bāṭil), i.e., it opposes it in usage and application. So it is the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality. So it is said: 'A false statement' and 'A false religion,' i.e., it did not correspond to reality. For your saying 'God is non-existent' has a linguistic relation, which is the affirmation of non-existence for God, while the real relation is the affirmation of existence. So the linguistic relation does not correspond to the real relation. Thus non-correspondence is falsehood.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining 'al-haqq' and its opposite 'al-bāṭil', and distinguishing 'al-haqq' from 'al-sidq'. It begins by explaining that 'al-haqq' applies to statements, beliefs, religions, and schools because they all contain the truth as an attribute inheres in the described. The passage then notes that 'al-sidq' is especially used for statements. It introduces two conceptual attributes of a judgment that corresponds to reality: (1) being a correspondence where reality is the standard (giving 'sidq'), and (2) being a correspondence where the judgment is the standard (giving 'haqq' in the verbal sense). The two are inseparable and essentially the same; the difference is only in the direction of correspondence. The passage emphasizes that reality is fixed and is measured against, not measured by, others. An example illustrates that the active participant (wazir) is the agent, not the sultan. Finally, it defines 'al-bāṭil' as the opposite: the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality, giving the example of saying 'God is non-existent' which does not correspond to the real existence of God.

Key Terms
الحق (al-haqq)
الصدق (al-sidq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-haqq and al-sidq are completely different concepts.
Correction: They both refer to correspondence with reality; the difference is mainly in usage: sidq is especially for statements, haqq is more general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, al-haqq applies to:
A) Only statements
B) Only beliefs
C) Statements, beliefs, religions, and schools
D) Only religions

Key Takeaway
Al-haqq applies to every correct judgment, whether in statements, beliefs, or legal rulings, while al-sidq is especially used for statements.

?
In your daily life, do you use the word 'truth' only for statements you make, or do you also recognize that truth applies to your beliefs and actions? Are your personal beliefs truly in accordance with reality (al-haqq)?
?
The passage teaches that different religious schools (madhahib) each contain truth. How does this affect your attitude towards differences of opinion among scholars? Do you genuinely accept that other valid rulings also contain truth, or do you dismiss them?

The Two Perspectives of Correspondence: Haqq and Sidq
Explaining the two conceptual attributes of a judgment corresponding to reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every judgment corresponding to reality has two conceptual attributes: one from the perspective of reality (sidq) and one from the perspective of the judgment (haqq).

Core Idea
Sidq means reality measures the judgment; haqq means the judgment measures reality.

The commentator now delves into a deeper analysis of correspondence. He explains that when a judgment matches reality, it can be viewed from two angles: making reality the standard (giving sidq) or making the judgment the standard (giving haqq). These two are inseparable, as illustrated by the example of the vizier and sultan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two attributes
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two distinct descriptions of correspondence: one with kasra and one with fatha.
Pay attention to the grammatical explanation of 'muṭābq' with different vowelings.
2
Understand the two directions of correspondence
First attribute: correspondence where reality is the standard → called sidq. Second attribute: where the judgment is the standard → called haqq.
3
Note the example of the wazir and sultan
The example illustrates that reality is like the sultan (active party) and the judgment like the vizier. We say 'The vizier sat with the sultan,' not the reverse, emphasizing that reality is the measure.
4
See why they are inseparable
The two attributes are inseparable because the correspondence is mutual; the difference is only in which side is taken as the standard.
This leads to the conclusion that haqq and sidq are essentially the same.

Do you not see that you say 'The vizier sat with the sultan,' and do not say 'The sultan sat with the vizier'? And its opposite is falsehood (al-bāṭil), i.e., it opposes it in usage and application. So it is the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality. So it is said: 'A false statement' and 'A false religion,' i.e., it did not correspond to reality. For your saying 'God is non-existent' has a linguistic relation, which is the affirmation of non-existence for God, while the real relation is the affirmation of existence. So the linguistic relation does not correspond to the real relation. Thus non-correspondence is falsehood.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining 'al-haqq' and its opposite 'al-bāṭil', and distinguishing 'al-haqq' from 'al-sidq'. It begins by explaining that 'al-haqq' applies to statements, beliefs, religions, and schools because they all contain the truth as an attribute inheres in the described. The passage then notes that 'al-sidq' is especially used for statements. It introduces two conceptual attributes of a judgment that corresponds to reality: (1) being a correspondence where reality is the standard (giving 'sidq'), and (2) being a correspondence where the judgment is the standard (giving 'haqq' in the verbal sense). The two are inseparable and essentially the same; the difference is only in the direction of correspondence. The passage emphasizes that reality is fixed and is measured against, not measured by, others. An example illustrates that the active participant (wazir) is the agent, not the sultan. Finally, it defines 'al-bāṭil' as the opposite: the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality, giving the example of saying 'God is non-existent' which does not correspond to the real existence of God.

Key Terms
المطابقة (al-muṭābaqa)
الواقع (al-wāqiʿ)
الحكم (al-ḥukm)
الصدق (al-sidq)
الحق (al-haqq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sidq and haqq refer to fundamentally different concepts.
Correction: They both refer to correspondence; the difference is in which is taken as the standard.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the attribute of correspondence called 'sidq'?
A) Because the judgment is the standard
B) Because reality is the standard
C) Because it applies only to statements
D) Because it means actualization

Key Takeaway
Sidq and haqq are two inseparable attributes of the same correspondence: sidq considers reality as the measure, haqq considers the judgment as the measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you evaluate your own beliefs by reality, or do you force reality to fit your preconceived notions? In what area of your life are you most likely to do the latter, and what would it cost you to surrender that attachment?
?
The passage says reality is the fixed measure that is not measured by others. Is there a reality in your life—a truth about yourself or your relationship with Allah—that you are trying to measure by your own judgments instead of submitting to it? How would you know?
?
When you speak, do you care more about being 'correct' (sidq) in the sense of matching facts, or about aligning your speech with what is truly 'right' (haqq) in the sense of conforming to the ultimate reality of Allah's commands? When these two conflict, which one weighs more heavily on your conscience?

The Opposite: Al-Bāṭil (Falsehood)
Defining al-bāṭil as non-correspondence with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that falsehood (al-bāṭil) is defined as the non-correspondence of a judgment to reality, and you will see an example.

Core Idea
Al-bāṭil is simply the lack of correspondence between a judgment and reality.

After defining truth and its attributes, the commentator turns to its opposite: falsehood. He defines al-bāṭil as the non-correspondence of a judgment to reality and gives a clear example: saying 'God is non-existent' is false because it does not match reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-bāṭil
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the concise definition.
2
Understand the definition
Al-bāṭil is defined as the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality. It is the opposite of haqq in all usages.
3
Analyze the example
The example: 'God is non-existent' – the linguistic relation (claim of non-existence) does not correspond to the real relation (existence of God). Thus it is false.
4
Notice the application to various judgments
Like haqq, falsehood applies to statements, religions, etc. The passage mentions 'a false statement' and 'a false religion'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والأديان) كقولك : الصلاة واجبة ، فثبوت الوجوب للصلاة دين ، وهذا الثبوت مشتمل على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على الصفة، وقوله : والمذاهب أي: النسب التامة التي ذهب إليها المجتهد، كقوله : الوتر مندوب، فثبوت الندب للوتر مذهب، أي: نسبة ذهب إليها المجتهد ، وهي مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته ، فعلمت أن العقائد والأديان والمذاهب مشتملة على الحق من اشتمال الموصوف على صفته ، بمعنى قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وأما اشتماله على الحق فمن اشتمال الدال على وصف المدلول . قوله: (يطلق على الأقوال والعقائد والأديان ... إلخ) بمعنى استعمال الحق في الكل على حد سواء، بخلاف الصدق فقد كثر استعماله في الأقوال خاصة. واعلم أن الحكم المطابق للواقع له صفتان اعتباریتان: كونه مطابقا بالكسر فيقال له الصدق ؛ لأنه الأصل الذي يجب اعتباره ووضع الاسم بإزائه ، فإن الأقرب إلى الطبع أن يجعل الواقع أصلا ، ويقاس عليه الحكم الذي يتعرف حاله. وكونه مطابقا للواقع بفتح الباء ويقال له الحق بالمعنى المصدري ؛ لأنه في الأصل بمعنى: التحقق ، والحكم في هذا الاعتبار جعل أصلا ثانيا حتى قيس به الواقع والصفتان متلازمتان ؛ لأن المفاعلة للمشاركة، ولا تفاوت بين الحق والصدق إلا بما تقدم، ويقال هذا كلام صدق أي: مطابق للواقع ، وهذا كلام حق أي مطابق للواقع، أي: إن ما أفاده الكلام مطابق للواقع ، فالأسهل أنهما شيء واحد وهو مطابق الخبر للواقع ، فالواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيره ولا يقاس على غيره، فتلاحظ أن غيره هل طابقه أو لا ، لا أنه هل طابق غيره أو لا وإن كانت المفاعلة من الجانبين ، ألا ترى أنك تقول : جالس الوزير السلطان ، ولا تقول: جالس السلطان الوزير الشنواني . وضده الباطل، أي: يضاده إطلاقا واستعمالا ، فهو عدم مطابقة الحكم الواقع ، فيقال: قول باطل ودين باطل ، أي : لم يطابق الواقع . فقولك : الله معدوم له نسبة كلامية ، وهي ثبوت العدم لله تعالى والنسبة الواقعية ثبوت الوجود، فالنسبة الكلامية غير مطابقة للنسبة الواقعية ، فعدم المطابقة هو الباطل. الشنواني .
His saying: (and the religions) like your saying: 'Prayer is obligatory.' So the affirmation of obligation for prayer is a religion, and this affirmation contains the truth (al-haqq) in the manner of the attribute containing the described. And his saying: 'and the schools' meaning the complete relations that the mujtahid adopted, like his saying: 'The witr is recommended.' So the affirmation of recommendation for witr is a school, i.e., a relation adopted by the mujtahid, and it contains the truth in the manner of the attribute containing its described. So you know that beliefs, religions, and schools contain the truth in the manner of the attribute containing its described, meaning the subsistence of the attribute in the described. As for its containing the truth, it is from the containing of the signifier for the description of the signified. His saying: 'It is applied to statements, beliefs, religions... etc.' meaning that the use of 'truth' in all is equal, contrary to 'truthfulness (al-sidq)' which is often used especially in statements. Know that the judgment corresponding to reality has two conceptual attributes: being a correspondence with a kasra, so it is called 'truthfulness' because it is the origin that must be considered and the name placed for it, for it is closer to nature to make reality the origin and measure the judgment against it to know its state. And being a correspondence with a fatha, so it is called 'the truth' in the verbal sense, because it originally means actualization, and the judgment in this consideration is made a second origin until reality is measured against it. The two attributes are inseparable because the mutafā‘ala is for sharing. There is no difference between truth and truthfulness except what has been mentioned. It is said 'This is truthful speech,' i.e., corresponding to reality, and 'This is true speech,' i.e., corresponding to reality, i.e., what the speech indicates is corresponding to reality. The easiest view is that they are one thing, namely the correspondence of the report to reality. For reality is something fixed in itself, measured against others and not measured by others. So you observe whether other things correspond to it or not, not whether it corresponds to others or not, even though the mutafā‘ala is from both sides. Do you not see that you say 'The vizier sat with the sultan,' and do not say 'The sultan sat with the vizier'? And its opposite is falsehood (al-bāṭil), i.e., it opposes it in usage and application. So it is the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality. So it is said: 'A false statement' and 'A false religion,' i.e., it did not correspond to reality. Definition of Sahabi and Its Conditions
The commentator defines the term sahabi and explains the conditions of companionship, with a preliminary linguistic clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a Ṣaḥābī (Companion) according to the commentators, including the linguistic clarification of the term ṣaḥb and the four conditions of companionship, with special attention to the nuance that discernment (tamyīz) is not a condition for the status itself.

Core Idea
A Ṣaḥābī is defined by meeting the Prophet, believing in him, and dying upon Islam; discernment is not required for companionship.

This lesson unpacks the definition of a Companion from the commentary. The passage begins with a linguistic note on the word 'ṣaḥb' and then presents the technical definition. Each condition is explained in detail, clarifying misconceptions such as the requirement of discernment. By the end, you will have a clear understanding of who qualifies as a Companion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the passage has two parts: a linguistic clarification of the word 'ṣaḥb' and the definition of Ṣaḥābī with its conditions.
2
Identify the definition of Ṣaḥābī
Find the sentence that defines Ṣaḥābī: 'وَالصَّحابي : مَن لقيه صلى الله عليه وسلم مميّزا مؤمناً به ومات على الإسلام'. Note the conditions: meeting, being discerning, believing, and dying upon Islam.
3
Understand each condition
Use the footnotes to understand each condition. Note that 'meeting' includes all forms of encounter, even without speech. 'Believing' excludes disbelievers. 'Dying upon Islam' ensures continuity. 'Discerning' is actually not a condition for companionship but for narration.
4
Reflect on the linguistic part
The first part of the passage explains that 'ṣaḥb' is a collective noun, not a true plural. This grammatical point sets the stage for understanding the term used in the matn.
This is less central to the definition but helps in understanding the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الآل والصحب والحزب
(وَ) على (صَحْبه) أي: أصحابه (١) (ε)
والصَّحابي : مَن لقيه (٢) صلى الله عليه وسلم مميّزا(٣) مؤمناً به ومات على الإسلام (٤)؛
(١) تفسير الصَّحْب بالأصحاب يوهم أَنَّه يعتبره جمعاً لصاحب، وهو مذهب أبي الحسن صاحب، ونظيره شرب وشارب، وتجر وتاجر، والتحقيق قول سيبويه إمام أهل هذه الصناعة، ذهب إلى أن صحباً اسم جمع لصاحب؛ لأنَّه على زِنةٍ من زنات جموع التكسير المعروفة. ومن هنا تعلم أن قول النُّحاة: (اسم الجمع: ما ليس له من لفظه، بل له واحد من معناه مبني على الغالب والكثير، وأنَّ مدار الفرق بين الجمع واسم الجمع على أن يكون على زنة من أوزان الجموع أولا، فإن كان على زنتها فهو جمع، وإن لم يكن فهو اسم جمع. محي.
(٢) قوله: (من لقيه) والمراد باللقاء: ما هو أعم من المجالسة، والمماشاة، ووصول أحدهما إلى الآخر، وإن لم يكالمه، ويدخل فيه برؤية أحدهما الآخر، سواء عرفه أو لا، والتعبير بـ(لقي) أولى من قول بعضهم رأى؛ لأنه يخرج ابن أم مكتوم ونحوه من العميان، وهم صحابة بلا تردد، ولقي في هذا التعريف كالجنس. الشنواني.
(٣) قوله: (مميزا) ليس بقيد على الصحيح، وقد عرفوا محمد بن أبي بكر صحابيا مع ولادته قبل وفاته صلى الله عليه بثلاثة أشهر، والتمييز إنما يشترط لتلقي الرواية فقط، فيدخل من بالتمر من الصبيان، والمجنون المحكوم بإسلامه فيما يظهر والنائم، فلا يشترط قصد ذلك الشخص الاجتماع، ولا معرفة أحدهما الآخر، و(مميزا) حال من فاعل (لقي). الشنواني.
(٤) قوله: (مؤمنا به) خرج من لقيه كافرا، ولو آمن به بعده كالتنوخي رسول هرقل. قوله: (به) هذا فصل ثان، يخرج من لقيه مؤمنا بغيره من الأنبياء، لكن هل يخرج من لقيه مؤمنا بأنه سيبعث ولم يدرك البعثة، فيه نظر! قلت: مال شيخ الإسلام إلى اعتبار لقيه له بعد نبوته، ونقل عن كلام ابن حجر ما يدل عليه. وقوله: (ومات على الإسلام) هذا ليس بشرط في أصل الصُّحبة، بل شرط في دوامها، غاية الأمر أن من زاده أراد تعريف الصحابي الذي ثبتت له الصحبة ولم تنقطع، ويخرج بالتعريف الأول من اجتمع به مؤمنا ومات مرتدا، كعبد الله بن خطل فليس بصحابي، ويدخل في التعريفين من عاد للإيمان وتوفي مؤمنا كعبد الله بن أبي سرج، وفائدتها: التسمية؛ فيسمى صحابيا، والكفاءة؛ فيكون كفؤا لبنت الصحابي، قال بعضهم: قلت ومن ذلك جعلُ مَن اجتمع به تابعيا، وعدم حنث الحالف على أنه صحابي، واشتهر أنها لا تعود عند المالكية، فحينئذ لا مانع من الرجوع فيه لمذهب الشافعي على ما كان يرتضيه بعض الأشياخ. الشنواني.
The meaning of Āl (family), Ṣaḥb (companions), and Ḥizb (party). (And) upon (his Ṣaḥb) meaning: his companions (1). And the Ṣaḥābī (companion) is: whoever met (2) him – صلى الله عليه وسلم – being discerning (3), believing in him, and died upon Islam (4). (1) Interpreting 'ṣaḥb' as 'aṣḥāb' gives the impression that it considers it a plural of ṣāḥib, which is the school of Abu al-Ḥasan al-Ṣāḥib, and its parallel is 'sharb' and 'shārib', 'tajar' and 'tājir'. The verification is the saying of Sībawayh, the imam of the people of this craft, who held that 'ṣaḥb' is a collective noun for 'ṣāḥib' because it is on a pattern of the known patterns of broken plurals. From here you learn that the statement of the grammarians: 'A collective noun is that which does not have a singular from its own word, but rather has a singular from its meaning' is based on what is predominant and frequent, and that the criterion of distinction between plural and collective noun is whether it is on a pattern of plural patterns or not; if it is on their pattern, it is a plural; if not, it is a collective noun. Muḥī. (2) His statement: 'whoever met him' – and by 'meeting' is meant what is more general than sitting together, walking together, and one reaching the other, even if he did not speak to him, and it includes one seeing the other, whether he knows him or not. And the expression 'met' is more appropriate than the saying of some: 'saw', because it excludes Ibn Umm Maktūm and the like of the blind, and they are companions without hesitation. And 'met' in this definition is like the genus. Al-Shanawānī. (3) His statement: 'discerning' – it is not a condition according to the correct opinion. Indeed, they considered Muḥammad ibn Abī Bakr a companion despite his birth three months before his death – صلى الله عليه وسلم. Discernment is only required for receiving narration. Thus, it includes infants fed with dates, the insane person judged to be Muslim apparently, and the sleeping person. So it is not required that the person intend the meeting, nor that one know the other. And 'discerning' is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'met'. Al-Shanawānī. (4) His statement: 'believing in him' excludes whoever met him as a disbeliever, even if he believed in him afterwards, like al-Tanūkhī, the messenger of Heraclius. His statement: 'in him' is a second partition, excluding whoever met him believing in a different prophet. But does it exclude whoever met him believing that he would be sent and did not witness the sending? There is consideration! I said: Shaykh al-Islām inclined to consider his meeting after his prophethood, and he transmitted from Ibn Ḥajar's words what indicates that. And his statement: 'and died upon Islam' – this is not a condition for the origin of companionship, but a condition for its continuity. At most, whoever added it intended the definition of the companion whose companionship is established and not cut off. By the first definition, whoever met him believing and died as an apostate is excluded, like ‘Abdullāh ibn Khaṭal, so he is not a companion. Both definitions include whoever returned to faith and died as a believer, like ‘Abdullāh ibn Abī Sarḥ. Its benefit is: naming – so he is called a companion; and compatibility – so he is compatible as a spouse for the daughter of a companion. Some said: I said, from that is the making of whoever met him a follower, and the non-oath-breaking of one who swore that he is a companion. And it is well-known that it does not return according to the Mālikīs. Therefore, there is no impediment to resorting in that to the Shāfi‘ī school according to what some teachers used to approve. Al-Shanawānī.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'Ṣaḥābī' (Companion) and explains its conditions, as part of a commentary on a matn about the meanings of Āl, Ṣaḥb, and Ḥizb. The commentator begins by clarifying the word 'ṣaḥb' in the matn. He notes that interpreting 'ṣaḥb' as 'aṣḥāb' might suggest it is a plural of 'ṣāḥib', which is the view of some grammarians. However, the correct view according to Sībawayh is that 'ṣaḥb' is a collective noun, not a true plural. The distinction between plural and collective noun depends on whether the word follows a known plural pattern. Then the commentator gives the definition: a Ṣaḥābī is anyone who met the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) while discerning, believing in him, and died upon Islam. Each condition is then explained in the footnotes. First, 'meeting' (liqā') is broader than just seeing; it includes any form of direct encounter, such as sitting together, walking together, or one reaching the other, even without conversation. The term 'met' is preferred over 'saw' to include blind companions like Ibn Umm Maktūm. Second, 'discerning' (mumayyiz) is not actually a condition for companionship itself, but only for receiving narration. Therefore, infants and the insane are included as companions if they had contact with the Prophet. Third, 'believing in him' (mu'minan bihī) excludes those who met him as disbelievers, even if they later believed, and also excludes those who believed in a previous prophet. There is debate about those who believed he would be sent but never witnessed his prophethood. The commentator notes that Shaykh al-Islām and Ibn Ḥajar inclined to consider meeting after prophethood as necessary. Fourth, 'died upon Islam' is not a condition for the origin of companionship but for its continuity. This means that someone who met the Prophet as a believer but died an apostate is not considered a companion permanently. Someone who apostatized but returned to faith before death is included. The benefit of this condition is determining who is called a companion and ensuring compatibility for marriage. The commentator also mentions that according to the Mālikī school, the attribute of companionship once lost does not return, but one may follow the Shāfi‘ī school on this point.

Key Terms
صحابي
صَحْب
لقاء
تمييز
مؤمنا به
مات على الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a person must be discerning (mumayyiz) at the time of meeting the Prophet to be considered a Companion.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that discernment (tamyīz) is only required for receiving narration, not for the status of companionship itself. Infants and the insane who met the Prophet are still Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is NOT a condition for being a Companion (Ṣaḥābī)?
A) Meeting the Prophet (peace be upon him)
B) Being a believer in him at the time of meeting
C) Being discerning (mumayyiz) at the time of meeting
D) Dying upon Islam

Key Takeaway
The definition of a Ṣaḥābī requires meeting the Prophet, believing in him, and dying upon Islam; discernment is not a condition, only for narration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions met the Prophet physically; you have only his words through revelation and hadith. When you read the Quran or a hadith, do you truly feel you are 'meeting' the Prophet? What would change in your life if you approached every statement of his with the same reverence as if he were standing before you?
?
The condition of 'dying upon Islam' distinguishes a true companion from one who merely associated with the Prophet. Are you living today in a way that you are certain you will die upon Islam? What is one concrete habit you must change to ensure your final state is that of faith?
?
The source mentions that infants and the insane are included as companions because discernment is not required. This shows that companionship with the Prophet is a gift, not earned by merit. How does this humble you? Do you sometimes feel you must 'earn' a connection to the Prophet through your deeds, forgetting that his companionship was a mercy? How can you actively receive that mercy today?

Extending the Definition of Companion: Including Prophets and Angels
Commentator extends definition of Ṣaḥābī to include prophets, angels, and defines ḥizb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator extends the definition of Ṣaḥābī to include the blind, prophets, and angels, and what is meant by 'Ḥizb'.

Core Idea
Companionship is defined by meeting, not seeing or acquaintance, so it includes all who met the Prophet, even prophets and angels.

In this passage, the commentator applies the earlier definition of a companion (ṣaḥābī) as 'whoever met the Prophet' to specific cases that might seem problematic. He argues that the definition does not require visual sight or personal acquaintance, thus including blind individuals like Ibn Umm Maktūm, and even prophets like Jesus and angels. He also clarifies the meaning of 'ḥizb' as a group. This expands our understanding of who can be considered a companion of the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation, noting the key terms: لقاء (meeting), تعارف (acquaintance), حزب (group).
Pay attention to the footnote numbers as they indicate scholarly commentary.
2
Identify the cases included
The commentator lists three categories: the blind (Ibn Umm Maktūm), prophets (Jesus, al-Khiḍr, Elijah), and angels. Why are each included?
Note that the inclusion of the blind is due to using 'meeting' instead of 'seeing'.
3
Examine the justification
The justification is that meeting (liqāʾ) does not require visual sight or mutual acquaintance. The commentator argues that companionship (ṣuḥba) is compatible with prophethood and angelic nature.
This is a key point: acquaintance (taʿāruf) is not a condition.
4
Understand the footnote criticisms
The footnotes discuss a weak opinion about angels being obligated by the Prophet's law. Note that this view is contested; the stronger position is that the Prophet's message is for jinn and humans only.
The footnotes also clarify that 'because they are obligated' may mean 'accompanying their obligation'.
5
Define 'Ḥizb'
The final line defines 'Ḥizb' as 'his group' (jamāʿatuhu), referring to the Prophet's party or followers.
This is a straightforward definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيدخلُ ابن أم مكتوم (١) ونحوه مِن العُميان؛ وعيسى والخضر وإلياس عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام؛ لحصول اللقاء؛ ولأنَّه لا يُشترط فيه التَّعارف؛ إذْ لا تنافي بين مقام الصحبة والنُّبوة والمَلَكيَّة، فعيسى آخرُ الصَّحابة موتا، والملائكة صحابةٌ باقُونَ إلى الآن؛ لتكليفهم بشريعته (٢).
(١) ابن أم مكتوم: اسمه عبد الله، وهو أحد مؤذني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، والفاء في قوله (فيدخل ابن أم مكتوم) للتفريع، ودخول ابن أم مكتوم مفرع في تعريف الصحابي بما ذكر حيث عبر الشارح بقوله: (من لقيه) دون أن يعبر بمن رآه كما عبر به بعضهم، فإنَّ ظاهره أن الرُّؤية بصريّة، فإن كانت علمية استوى التعريفان في اشتمالهما على ابن أم مكتوم ونحوه. محي.
(٢) هذا مبني على مذهب ضعيف، وهو أنَّ الملائكة ممن أرسل إليهم الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، وأنهم مكلفون، والأقرب إلى الحق أنَّ رسالته للثقلين وهما الجن والإنس فقط، ولو سُلَّم أَنَّه مرسل إلى الملائكة أيضًا لم يسلم أنهم مُكلفون، لأنَّ التكليف إنَّما يكون بما يكون فيه كُلفة ومشقة، وطاعة الملائكة جبلية لا مشقة على واحد منهم في القيام بها. محي. وعند الشنواني: قوله: لتكليفهم بشريعته) الكلام بمعنى مع، أي: باقون مع تكليفهم بشريعته؛ لأن الصحبة لا تتوقف على التكليف، وهو أحد القولين عندهم في الملائكة، وهما فيما عدا الإيمان؛ لأنه ضروري، والراجح في مذهب الشارح أنهم في الشريعة ولكن تفصيل ذلك لا نعلمه، فلا ندري هل هم مكلفون بالصلاة وغيرها مما كلفنا به، والراجح عند الشافعية أنّ إرساله لهم تشريف، وأن طاعتهم جبليّة، والتكليف إنما يكون بما فيه كلفة.
(و) على (حزبه) أي: جماعته.
So it includes Ibn Umm Maktūm (1) and the like of the blind; and Jesus, al-Khiḍr, and Elijah (peace be upon them) due to the occurrence of meeting; and because acquaintance is not required; since there is no contradiction between the station of companionship and prophethood and angelic nature. Thus, Jesus is the last of the companions to die, and the angels are companions remaining until now, because they are obligated by his sharīʿah (2). (1) Ibn Umm Maktūm: his name is ʿAbdullāh, and he was one of the muʾadhdhins of the Messenger of Allāh, صلى الله عليه وسلم. The fāʾ in his statement (so it includes Ibn Umm Maktūm) is for conclusion, and the inclusion of Ibn Umm Maktūm is derived from the definition of the companion as mentioned, since the commentator expressed with his statement: 'whoever met him' without expressing with 'whoever saw him' as some did. For its apparent meaning is that 'seeing' is visual; if it were cognitive, the two definitions would be equal in including Ibn Umm Maktūm and the like. Muḥī. (2) This is based on a weak opinion, which is that the angels are among those to whom the Messenger, صلى الله عليه وسلم, was sent, and that they are obligated. The closest to the truth is that his message is for the two weighty things (thaqalayn), namely jinn and humans only. And even if it is conceded that he was sent to the angels as well, it is not conceded that they are obligated, because obligation is only concerning that which involves burden and difficulty, and the obedience of angels is innate, with no difficulty for any of them in performing it. Muḥī. And according to al-Shanawānī: 'because they are obligated by his sharīʿah' means the word is with the meaning 'accompanying', i.e., remaining with their obligation by his sharīʿah; because companionship does not depend on obligation. And this is one of two opinions among them concerning the angels—those two opinions are other than faith, because it is necessary. The most preponderant in the school of the commentator is that they are in the sharīʿah, but we do not know its detail, so we do not know whether they are obligated with prayer and other than it which we are obligated. The most preponderant according to the Shāfiʿīs is that his sending to them is an honor, and that their obedience is innate, and obligation is only concerning that which involves difficulty. (And) upon (his Ḥizb) meaning: his group.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the definition of Ṣaḥābī, applying it to specific cases and then defining 'Ḥizb'. The commentator states that the definition includes Ibn Umm Maktūm, a blind companion, because the term 'meeting' (liqā') was used instead of 'seeing' (ruʾya). A marginal note explains that had 'seeing' been used literally (visual sight), it would exclude the blind; but if it were interpreted cognitively, both definitions would include them. The commentator then extends the definition to include prophets such as Jesus, al-Khiḍr, and Elijah, because they met the Prophet Muḥammad, and acquaintance is not required for companionship. Jesus is considered the last companion to die (since he will descend at the end of time), and angels are also considered companions because they met the Prophet and some argue they are obligated by his law. However, a marginal note criticizes this view as weak, stating that the Prophet's message is only for jinn and humans, and that angels' obedience is innate, so they are not obligated in the same way. Another marginal note clarifies that the phrase 'because they are obligated by his sharīʿah' may mean 'accompanying their obligation', not that obligation is necessary for companionship. The passage concludes with the matn line 'and upon his Ḥizb', which the commentator explains as 'his group' (jamāʿatuhu).

حزب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A companion must have seen the Prophet with their own eyes and personally known him.
Correction: The commentator uses 'meeting' (liqāʾ) instead of 'seeing' (ruʾya), and states that acquaintance is not required. Thus, blind individuals like Ibn Umm Maktūm and prophets who met the Prophet are included.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator include Jesus (ʿĪsā) among the companions?
A) Because Jesus was a prophet and all prophets are companions.
B) Because Jesus met the Prophet Muhammad in person during his earthly life.
C) Because the definition of companion is based on meeting, and Jesus met the Prophet during the ascension (miʿrāj) or will meet him at the end of time.
D) Because Jesus performed miracles.

Key Takeaway
The definition of Ṣaḥābī is broad: anyone who met the Prophet, even if they did not see him or know him personally, including prophets and angels, and 'Ḥizb' means his group.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have never seen the Prophet, yet the definition of ṣuḥba here is based on meeting, not sight. Do you meet him daily through his sunna, or have you let the 'meeting' become a distant memory? What will you change today to make your meeting with him real again?
?
The blind companion Ibn Umm Maktūm is included despite his lack of sight—his companionship is based on meeting alone. What physical or spiritual 'blindness' might be preventing you from feeling like a true companion of the Prophet? Will you ask Allah tonight to grant you the status of companionship through your sincere meeting with his message?
?
Angels, who are sinless, are called companions of the Prophet because they met him. You sin every day, yet the Prophet is more merciful than any angel. If you truly met him through his teaching, would you not love what he loves and hate what he hates? Are you willing to let go of one sin this week to prove that you are truly among his ḥizb?

Al-Raghib's Definition of Knowledge and Its Explanation
Al-Raghib defines knowledge as apprehension of a thing in its reality, with explanation and response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Raghib's definition of knowledge ('ilm), its scope, and how an objection regarding impossible objects is resolved.

Core Idea
Knowledge is apprehending a thing as it really is, encompassing both conceptualization and assent.

Al-Raghib's definition of knowledge is a classic starting point in Islamic epistemology. The passage presents his concise definition, then unpacks what 'apprehension' means and why it covers both forming concepts and affirming truths. Finally, it addresses a subtle objection about whether the definition can handle knowledge of impossible things—showing the precision required in theological terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its clarification
Start with al-Raghib's definition and the commentator's note that 'apprehension' here means either conceptualization or assent. This sets the semantic range.
Notice that the definition is abstracted from the issue of faculty.
2
Identify the objection from al-Mawaqif
Find the objection: the word 'thing' seems to exclude impossible objects like a second god, since they are not considered 'things' by consensus.
This objection tests the universality of the definition.
3
Study the response
The answer is that 'thing' is used linguistically to include even impossible objects. Understand why linguistic inclusion matters for this definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الرّاغب : العِلم : إدراك الشَّيء بحقيقته. اعلم أن غرضه تفسير العلم بقطع النظر عن كونه تصح إرادته هنا أو لا، إذ اعتبارُ المَلكة لا يصح هنا إلا باعتبار ما ينشأ عنها وهو الإدراك، أي التصديق. فقوله: (إدراك الشيء بحقيقته) أي تصور الشيء أو التصديق به. واعترض في «المواقف» على التعبير بالشيء بأنه يخرج علم المستحيل فإنه ليس شيئا من الأشياء اتفاقا، بخلاف المعدوم الممكن، وأجاب بأنه شيء لغة.
Al-Raghib said: Knowledge: apprehension of a thing in its reality. Know that his purpose is to interpret knowledge regardless of whether its intended meaning is valid here or not, for consideration of the faculty is not valid here except by considering what arises from it, which is apprehension, i.e., assent. So his saying: (apprehension of a thing in its reality) means conceptualization of the thing or assent to it. And an objection was raised in 'al-Mawaqif' against the expression 'thing' that it excludes knowledge of the impossible, for it is not a thing among things by agreement, unlike the possible non-existent, and the answer was that it is a thing linguistically.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the definition of knowledge ('ilm) given by al-Raghib. It begins by quoting his definition: 'Knowledge is the apprehension of a thing in its reality.' The commentator then clarifies that this definition is intended to interpret knowledge abstractly, regardless of whether the specific meaning (such as the faculty of knowledge) is intended here. He explains that the term 'apprehension' (idrāk) here refers to either conceptualization (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), so the definition covers both types of knowledge. Then an objection from the book 'al-Mawaqif' is mentioned: using the word 'thing' (shay') excludes knowledge of impossible objects (al-mustaḥīl), because impossible things are not considered 'things' by consensus, unlike non-existent possible things. The response is that 'thing' applies to impossible things as well according to linguistic usage. This demonstrates the care taken to ensure the definition is comprehensive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of knowledge as 'apprehension of a thing in its reality' cannot apply to impossible objects because they are not real things.
Correction: Al-Raghib's definition uses 'thing' in a linguistic sense that includes even impossible objects, so it covers knowledge of them.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Raghib's definition of knowledge covers both conceptualization and assent, and the term 'thing' is used broadly to include even impossible objects, ensuring universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Raghib's definition of knowledge includes assent (tasdiq). Is your knowledge of Allah merely conceptual, or does it move you to heartfelt conviction that shapes your actions?
?
When you encounter a difficult theological concept like 'the impossible is a thing', do you pause to understand it fully, or do you dismiss it as irrelevant? How might your spiritual journey change if you committed to apprehending every truth as it really is?

The Definition of Absolute Knowledge: Perception of a Thing According to Its Reality
Commentator defines absolute knowledge and explains its terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational definition of absolute knowledge in Islamic theology and the intended meaning of its key terms.

Core Idea
Absolute knowledge is defined as the perception of a thing exactly as it is in reality.

The commentator begins by establishing the standard definition of knowledge used in kalam: 'perception of a thing according to its reality.' This definition is not arbitrary—it is chosen to reflect the original linguistic meaning while excluding misconceptions. In this lesson, we unpack the terms 'perception,' 'thing,' and 'according to its reality' to see how they precisely delineate what counts as true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its immediate explanation
The passage opens with the definition and notes that 'perception' (idrak) is what is intended here. Notice how the commentator emphasizes that this is the original meaning of the word 'ilm.
The definition is 'perception of a thing according to its reality.'
2
Understand why 'perception' is used instead of other terms
The commentator clarifies that 'perception' is evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it. Reflect on why this word was chosen over alternatives like 'acquaintance.'
The original meaning of 'ilm is perception.
3
Differentiate between the three uses of 'ilm
The passage mentions that 'ilm is also used technically for 'codified rules' and for the 'faculty' (malaka). Distinguish these from the original definition.
These other usages are derived from the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصل كلمة وبعد» وتعريف مطلق العلم والجهل إدرا (۱) الشَّيء بحقيقته(٢). وهو كقول للفظ (۱) قوله: (إدراك) هذا هو المراد هنا ; بدليل الحكم عليه بالتَّحتُم، وهو المعنى الأصلي العلم; فإنه مصدر «علم» ويطلق حقيقة عُرفية على القواعد المُدونة وعلى الملكة كما يأتي للارتباط التسببي ، وتفسير العلم بالإدراك يقتضي تعدده بتعدد المعلوم ; كما إذا فُسر بالصورة الحاصلة في النفس بناء على أنَّ العلم عين المعلوم بمعنى أنَّ الشيء من حيث حصوله في الخارج .. معلوم ، ومن حيثُ حصوله في الذهن .. عِلمٌ ، وأما إن فُسر بالملكة فالأظهر عدم التعدد، وقد حكى الخلاف في هذه المسألة المصنف في شرحه وهو مشهور ، وأما العلم القديم فلم يقل بتعدده إلا الصعلوكي كما سيأتي ، وعدل الشارح عن قول الباقلاني : العلم معرفة معلوم.. لما أورده عليه العضد في «المواقف» من الدور; حيث أخذ المشتق في تعريف المشتق منه ، وإن أجيب بأنا نريد بالمعلوم ذات الشيء لا المعنى الاشتقاقي، نعم فيه فائدة ترادف العلم والمعرفة خلافا لمن خص العلم بالكليات أو المركبات، والمعرفة بالجزئيات أو البسائط ، ويوهمه قول النحاة : علم العرفان يتعدى لمفعول واحد، والحق كما قاله الرضي: : أنه مجرد فرق في الاستعمال فقط ، وخلافا لمن قال: المعرفة تستدعي سبق جهل فلذا لا تطلق على علم الله تعالى، قال السيد : في شرح المواقف» إجماعا لا لغة ولا اصطلاحا .اهـ. والحق أن عدم الإطلاق لعدم التوقيف على أن بعضهم جوزها لما ورد (تعرف إلى الله في الرخاء يعرفك في الشدة وإن احتمل المشاكلة أو المجازاة. الأمير. (۲) قوله: (قال الراغب ... الخ ) اعلم أن غرضه تفسير العلم بقطع النظر عن كونه تصح إرادته هنا أو لا ، إذ اعتبارُ المَلكة لا يصح هنا إلا باعتبار ما ينشأ عنها وهو الإدراك ، أي : التصديق. فقوله: (إدراك الشيء بحقيقته ) أي : تصور الشيء أو التصديق به ، ولما كان قوله إدراك الشيء يصدق بإدراكه على ما هو به مطلقا، وعلى ما هو في الاعتقاد دون الواقع ، وليس بصحيح ; لأنهما من أقسام الجهل أتى بما يخرجهما بقوله بحقيقته ، أي : إدراك الشيء في حال كونه ملتبسا بحقيقته أي بحاله التي هو عليها في الواقع ، كما هو المتبادر من قوله بحقيقته، كإدراك الإنسان أنه حيوان ناطق، والمتبادر من هذا التعريف أنه تعريف للعلم التصوري ; لأنه الذي يتعلق بالحقائق ، فهو تفسير لأحد نوعي العلم، وهذا هو الصواب، وهذا المعنى المراد هنا بدليل الحكم عليه بالتحكّم ، وهو المعنى الأصلي للفظ العلم، فإنه مصدر علم، هو ويطلق حقيقة عرفية على القواعد المدونة وعلى الملكة ، واعترض في «المواقف» على التعبير بالشيء ; بأنه يخرج علم المستحيل فإنه ليس شيئا من الأشياء اتفاقا، بخلاف المعدوم الممكن ، وأجاب بأنه شيء لغة. الشنواني .
The origin of the word 'and after' and the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance: 'Perception of the thing according to its reality.' (1) His statement: ('Perception') – this is what is intended here, evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it, and it is the original meaning of knowledge; for it is the source of 'know' and it is used in a customary technical sense for codified rules and for the faculty, as will come, due to causal connection. And interpreting knowledge as perception implies its multiplicity by the multiplicity of the known; as when it is interpreted as the form occurring in the soul, based on the fact that knowledge is identical to the known in the sense that the thing, in terms of its occurrence in the external, is 'known', and in terms of its occurrence in the mind, is 'knowledge'. But if interpreted as the faculty, the apparent view is non-multiplicity. The author has related the disagreement on this issue in his commentary, and it is well-known. As for eternal knowledge, only al-Sa'luki said it is multiple, as will come. The commentator deviated from al-Baqillani's statement: 'Knowledge is acquaintance with a known thing' due to the objection of al-'Adud in al-Mawaqif of circularity, where he took the derivative in defining that from which it is derived. Though it is answered that we mean by 'known' the essence of the thing, not the derivative meaning. Indeed, there is a benefit: synonymy of 'knowledge' and 'acquaintance', contrary to those who restrict 'knowledge' to universals or compounds, and 'acquaintance' to particulars or simples. The grammarians' statement 'the verb 'to know' (of acquaintance) takes one object' may imply this, but the truth, as al-Radi said, is that it is merely a difference in usage only. And contrary to those who said: 'acquaintance' requires prior ignorance, so it is not applied to God's knowledge. Al-Sayyid said in Sharh al-Mawaqif: 'By consensus, neither linguistically nor terminologically.' The truth is that non-application is due to the lack of textual authorization, though some permitted it because of the hadith: 'Know God in ease, and He will know you in hardship' although it may be interpreted as similarity or retribution. (2) His statement: ('al-Raghib said...') Know that his purpose is to interpret knowledge regardless of whether it can be intended here or not, for consideration of the faculty is not valid here except in terms of what arises from it, which is perception, i.e., affirmation. So his statement: ('Perception of a thing according to its reality') means: conceptualization of the thing or affirmation of it. And since his statement 'perception of a thing' is true of perceiving it absolutely as it is, and of what is in belief without reality, and that is not correct – because they are divisions of ignorance – he brought what excludes them by his statement 'according to its reality', i.e., perception of the thing in the state of being clothing with its reality, i.e., its state as it is in reality, as is understood from his expression 'according to its reality', like perceiving that a human is a rational animal. And what is understood from this definition is that it is a definition of conceptual knowledge, because it pertains to realities. So it is an interpretation of one of the two types of knowledge. And this is correct. And this meaning is intended here, evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it. And it is the original meaning of the word 'knowledge', for it is the source of 'know', and it is used in a customary technical sense for codified rules and for the faculty. And objection was raised in al-Mawaqif to the expression 'thing' because it excludes knowledge of the impossible, for it is not a thing among things by agreement, unlike the possible non-existent. And it was answered that it is a thing linguistically. (al-Shanwani).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of absolute knowledge as 'perception of a thing according to its reality.' The commentator begins by explaining that the term 'perception' (إدراك) is what is intended here, as evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it, and that it is the original meaning of the word 'knowledge.' He then discusses whether knowledge is multiple or single: if interpreted as perception, it would be multiple according to the multiplicity of known things; if interpreted as a faculty (ملكة), the apparent view is non-multiplicity. He notes that the author has related the disagreement on this issue in his commentary. He then mentions that al-Baqillani defined knowledge as 'acquaintance with a known thing,' but the commentator deviated due to the objection of circularity in al-Mawaqif, though an answer is given that 'known' refers to the essence, not the derivative meaning. The commentator then discusses the relationship between 'knowledge' (علم) and 'acquaintance' (معرفة), stating that they are synonymous, contrary to some who restrict each to different types of objects. He also addresses the claim that 'acquaintance' requires prior ignorance and therefore cannot be applied to God's knowledge, concluding that the non-application is due to lack of textual authorization, though some permit it based on a hadith. Finally, he explains the phrase 'according to its reality' (بحقيقته) to exclude perceptions that are not in accordance with reality, which would be ignorance, and clarifies that this definition is for conceptual knowledge (علم تصوري). He also addresses an objection about the word 'thing' excluding the impossible, and the answer that it is a thing linguistically.

Key Terms
علم
إدراك
الشيء
بحقيقته
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what evidence indicates that 'perception' (idrak) is the intended meaning in the definition?
A) It is the original meaning of 'ilm.
B) The judgment of necessity upon it.
C) It is used for codified rules.
D) It is the faculty.

Key Takeaway
The definition of absolute knowledge as 'perception of a thing according to its reality' is rooted in the original linguistic meaning of 'ilm, and is distinguished from its technical usages for codified rules and the faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah and His creation, do you truly seek to perceive things as they are, or do you settle for comfortable assumptions? What concrete step will you take today to align your learning with this definition of knowledge?
?
The commentator says that the original meaning of 'ilm is perception. Is your pursuit of Islamic knowledge merely accumulating information, or are you training your heart and mind to perceive reality as it truly is? What will you change?
?
You may think you know certain truths about faith, but have you perceived them according to their reality, or only accepted them by imitation? Choose one belief you hold and commit to re-examining it with the goal of perceiving its reality—what will you do this week?
