Multiplicity of Knowledge and the Distinction Between 'Ilm and Ma'rifah
Commentator discusses multiplicity of knowledge and synonymy of 'ilm and ma'rifah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether knowledge is multiple or one, and the relationship between the terms 'ilm and ma'rifah.

Core Idea
Knowledge may be multiple if it is perception, but single if it is a faculty, and 'ilm and ma'rifah are essentially synonymous.

The commentator now delves into a key debate: does knowledge become multiple as the objects of knowledge multiply? The answer depends on whether we interpret knowledge as perception (which would be multiple) or as a faculty (which is single). He also addresses the relationship between 'ilm and ma'rifah, correcting the misconception that they refer to different types of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the implication of interpreting knowledge as perception
The passage states that if knowledge is perception, then it would be multiple according to the multiplicity of known things. Imagine how each known object corresponds to a distinct perception.
This is based on the view that knowledge is the form of the object in the mind.
2
Contrast with the faculty interpretation
The commentator notes that if knowledge is interpreted as a faculty (malaka), the apparent view is that it is not multiple. Consider why a faculty might be seen as a single thing.
Eternal knowledge is an exception, only al-Sa'luki said it is multiple.
3
Examine the deviation from al-Baqillani's definition
The commentator moves to al-Baqillani's definition 'knowledge is acquaintance with a known thing' and the objection of circularity (dawr). How does the commentator respond?
The response is that 'known' refers to the essence, not the derivative.
4
Learn the synonymy of 'ilm and ma'rifah
The passage discusses the false distinction that 'ilm is for universals and ma'rifah for particulars, and clarifies that they are synonymous in usage, as al-Radi said.
The grammarians' rule about transitivity is just a difference in usage.

أصل كلمة وبعد» وتعريف مطلق العلم والجهل إدرا (۱) الشَّيء بحقيقته(٢). وهو كقول للفظ (۱) قوله: (إدراك) هذا هو المراد هنا ; بدليل الحكم عليه بالتَّحتُم، وهو المعنى الأصلي العلم; فإنه مصدر «علم» ويطلق حقيقة عُرفية على القواعد المُدونة وعلى الملكة كما يأتي للارتباط التسببي ، وتفسير العلم بالإدراك يقتضي تعدده بتعدد المعلوم ; كما إذا فُسر بالصورة الحاصلة في النفس بناء على أنَّ العلم عين المعلوم بمعنى أنَّ الشيء من حيث حصوله في الخارج .. معلوم ، ومن حيثُ حصوله في الذهن .. عِلمٌ ، وأما إن فُسر بالملكة فالأظهر عدم التعدد، وقد حكى الخلاف في هذه المسألة المصنف في شرحه وهو مشهور ، وأما العلم القديم فلم يقل بتعدده إلا الصعلوكي كما سيأتي ، وعدل الشارح عن قول الباقلاني : العلم معرفة معلوم.. لما أورده عليه العضد في «المواقف» من الدور; حيث أخذ المشتق في تعريف المشتق منه ، وإن أجيب بأنا نريد بالمعلوم ذات الشيء لا المعنى الاشتقاقي، نعم فيه فائدة ترادف العلم والمعرفة خلافا لمن خص العلم بالكليات أو المركبات، والمعرفة بالجزئيات أو البسائط ، ويوهمه قول النحاة : علم العرفان يتعدى لمفعول واحد، والحق كما قاله الرضي: : أنه مجرد فرق في الاستعمال فقط ، وخلافا لمن قال: المعرفة تستدعي سبق جهل فلذا لا تطلق على علم الله تعالى، قال السيد : في شرح المواقف» إجماعا لا لغة ولا اصطلاحا .اهـ. والحق أن عدم الإطلاق لعدم التوقيف على أن بعضهم جوزها لما ورد (تعرف إلى الله في الرخاء يعرفك في الشدة وإن احتمل المشاكلة أو المجازاة. الأمير. (۲) قوله: (قال الراغب ... الخ ) اعلم أن غرضه تفسير العلم بقطع النظر عن كونه تصح إرادته هنا أو لا ، إذ اعتبارُ المَلكة لا يصح هنا إلا باعتبار ما ينشأ عنها وهو الإدراك ، أي : التصديق. فقوله: (إدراك الشيء بحقيقته ) أي : تصور الشيء أو التصديق به ، ولما كان قوله إدراك الشيء يصدق بإدراكه على ما هو به مطلقا، وعلى ما هو في الاعتقاد دون الواقع ، وليس بصحيح ; لأنهما من أقسام الجهل أتى بما يخرجهما بقوله بحقيقته ، أي : إدراك الشيء في حال كونه ملتبسا بحقيقته أي بحاله التي هو عليها في الواقع ، كما هو المتبادر من قوله بحقيقته، كإدراك الإنسان أنه حيوان ناطق، والمتبادر من هذا التعريف أنه تعريف للعلم التصوري ; لأنه الذي يتعلق بالحقائق ، فهو تفسير لأحد نوعي العلم، وهذا هو الصواب، وهذا المعنى المراد هنا بدليل الحكم عليه بالتحكّم ، وهو المعنى الأصلي للفظ العلم، فإنه مصدر علم، هو ويطلق حقيقة عرفية على القواعد المدونة وعلى الملكة ، واعترض في «المواقف» على التعبير بالشيء ; بأنه يخرج علم المستحيل فإنه ليس شيئا من الأشياء اتفاقا، بخلاف المعدوم الممكن ، وأجاب بأنه شيء لغة. الشنواني .
The origin of the word 'and after' and the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance: 'Perception of the thing according to its reality.' (1) His statement: ('Perception') – this is what is intended here, evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it, and it is the original meaning of knowledge; for it is the source of 'know' and it is used in a customary technical sense for codified rules and for the faculty, as will come, due to causal connection. And interpreting knowledge as perception implies its multiplicity by the multiplicity of the known; as when it is interpreted as the form occurring in the soul, based on the fact that knowledge is identical to the known in the sense that the thing, in terms of its occurrence in the external, is 'known', and in terms of its occurrence in the mind, is 'knowledge'. But if interpreted as the faculty, the apparent view is non-multiplicity. The author has related the disagreement on this issue in his commentary, and it is well-known. As for eternal knowledge, only al-Sa'luki said it is multiple, as will come. The commentator deviated from al-Baqillani's statement: 'Knowledge is acquaintance with a known thing' due to the objection of al-'Adud in al-Mawaqif of circularity, where he took the derivative in defining that from which it is derived. Though it is answered that we mean by 'known' the essence of the thing, not the derivative meaning. Indeed, there is a benefit: synonymy of 'knowledge' and 'acquaintance', contrary to those who restrict 'knowledge' to universals or compounds, and 'acquaintance' to particulars or simples. The grammarians' statement 'the verb 'to know' (of acquaintance) takes one object' may imply this, but the truth, as al-Radi said, is that it is merely a difference in usage only. And contrary to those who said: 'acquaintance' requires prior ignorance, so it is not applied to God's knowledge. Al-Sayyid said in Sharh al-Mawaqif: 'By consensus, neither linguistically nor terminologically.' The truth is that non-application is due to the lack of textual authorization, though some permitted it because of the hadith: 'Know God in ease, and He will know you in hardship' although it may be interpreted as similarity or retribution. (2) His statement: ('al-Raghib said...') Know that his purpose is to interpret knowledge regardless of whether it can be intended here or not, for consideration of the faculty is not valid here except in terms of what arises from it, which is perception, i.e., affirmation. So his statement: ('Perception of a thing according to its reality') means: conceptualization of the thing or affirmation of it. And since his statement 'perception of a thing' is true of perceiving it absolutely as it is, and of what is in belief without reality, and that is not correct – because they are divisions of ignorance – he brought what excludes them by his statement 'according to its reality', i.e., perception of the thing in the state of being clothing with its reality, i.e., its state as it is in reality, as is understood from his expression 'according to its reality', like perceiving that a human is a rational animal. And what is understood from this definition is that it is a definition of conceptual knowledge, because it pertains to realities. So it is an interpretation of one of the two types of knowledge. And this is correct. And this meaning is intended here, evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it. And it is the original meaning of the word 'knowledge', for it is the source of 'know', and it is used in a customary technical sense for codified rules and for the faculty. And objection was raised in al-Mawaqif to the expression 'thing' because it excludes knowledge of the impossible, for it is not a thing among things by agreement, unlike the possible non-existent. And it was answered that it is a thing linguistically. (al-Shanwani).

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of absolute knowledge as 'perception of a thing according to its reality.' The commentator begins by explaining that the term 'perception' (إدراك) is what is intended here, as evidenced by the judgment of necessity upon it, and that it is the original meaning of the word 'knowledge.' He then discusses whether knowledge is multiple or single: if interpreted as perception, it would be multiple according to the multiplicity of known things; if interpreted as a faculty (ملكة), the apparent view is non-multiplicity. He notes that the author has related the disagreement on this issue in his commentary. He then mentions that al-Baqillani defined knowledge as 'acquaintance with a known thing,' but the commentator deviated due to the objection of circularity in al-Mawaqif, though an answer is given that 'known' refers to the essence, not the derivative meaning. The commentator then discusses the relationship between 'knowledge' (علم) and 'acquaintance' (معرفة), stating that they are synonymous, contrary to some who restrict each to different types of objects. He also addresses the claim that 'acquaintance' requires prior ignorance and therefore cannot be applied to God's knowledge, concluding that the non-application is due to lack of textual authorization, though some permit it based on a hadith. Finally, he explains the phrase 'according to its reality' (بحقيقته) to exclude perceptions that are not in accordance with reality, which would be ignorance, and clarifies that this definition is for conceptual knowledge (علم تصوري). He also addresses an objection about the word 'thing' excluding the impossible, and the answer that it is a thing linguistically.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ilm and ma'rifah refer to completely different types of knowledge (e.g., 'ilm for universals, ma'rifah for particulars).
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are synonymous in usage, and any difference is merely a matter of linguistic convention.

What is the main objection to al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge as 'acquaintance with a known thing'?
A) It implies multiplicity of knowledge.
B) It involves circularity by using a derivative in the definition.
C) It excludes knowledge of the impossible.
D) It restricts knowledge to universals.

Key Takeaway
The debate over the multiplicity of knowledge hinges on whether it is interpreted as perception (multiple) or as a faculty (single), and the terms 'ilm and ma'rifah are essentially synonymous despite attempts to distinguish them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learn many things about your faith—do you treat each piece of knowledge as a separate fact, or do you see them as parts of a single, integrated understanding that shapes your entire worldview? How might your approach change if you aimed for the latter?
?
The passage teaches that 'ilm and ma'rifah are essentially the same. But ask yourself: Is your knowledge of Allah merely a set of theoretical beliefs, or does it feel like personal acquaintance that affects your daily choices? If there is a gap, what will you do today to move from knowing about Him to truly knowing Him?
?

Clarifying 'According to Its Reality' and the Objection about 'Thing'
Commentator explains the phrase 'according to its reality' and addresses an objection about the term 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'according to its reality' excludes false perceptions and why 'thing' includes the possible non-existent but not the impossible.

Core Idea
The definition's phrase 'according to its reality' ensures only true knowledge counts, and 'thing' includes everything except the impossible.

The commentator now zooms in on the critical phrase 'according to its reality' (bi-haqiqatihi). Without this qualifier, the definition could include mistaken beliefs, which are actually ignorance. He also addresses an objection about the word 'thing' (al-shay'): does it exclude knowledge of the impossible? The answer reveals a subtle linguistic point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of 'according to its reality'
The passage explains that without this phrase, the definition would include perceptions that are not in reality, which are ignorance. The phrase ensures that only perceptions that match reality count as knowledge.
Example: perceiving a human as a rational animal is knowledge; believing a human is a stone is ignorance.
2
See how the definition applies to conceptual knowledge
The commentator notes that this definition is for conceptual knowledge (tasawwur), which deals with realities. Distinguish this from affirmational knowledge (tasdiq).
Conceptual knowledge is about grasping the essence of a thing.
3
Resolve the objection about 'thing'
The objection from al-Mawaqif is that 'thing' excludes the impossible, since it is not a thing. The answer is that 'thing' is defined linguistically to include it.
The objection distinguishes between possible non-existents (which are things) and the impossible (which is not).

The commentator then discusses the relationship between 'knowledge' (علم) and 'acquaintance' (معرفة), stating that they are synonymous, contrary to some who restrict each to different types of objects. He also addresses the claim that 'acquaintance' requires prior ignorance and therefore cannot be applied to God's knowledge, concluding that the non-application is due to lack of textual authorization, though some permit it based on a hadith. Finally, he explains the phrase 'according to its reality' (بحقيقته) to exclude perceptions that are not in accordance with reality, which would be ignorance, and clarifies that this definition is for conceptual knowledge (علم تصوري). He also addresses an objection about the word 'thing' excluding the impossible, and the answer that it is a thing linguistically.

Key Terms
إدراك
الشيء
بحقيقته
تصور
تصديق
مستحيل
معدوم
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'thing' automatically includes everything, including the impossible, without distinction.
Correction: The passage clarifies that technically, theologians do not consider the impossible a thing, but linguistically it can be considered a thing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of knowledge does the definition primarily apply to?
A) Affirmational knowledge (tasdiq)
B) Conceptual knowledge (tasawwur)
C) Knowledge of universals
D) Knowledge of particulars

Key Takeaway
The qualifier 'according to its reality' is essential to exclude false perceptions, and the term 'thing' in the definition is understood linguistically to encompass even the impossible, though theologians do not consider it a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of knowledge requires it to match reality. Have you ever believed something about your faith that you later found out was not based on reality? What was it, and how did you correct it?
?
The commentator warns that without the qualifier 'according to its reality', the definition would include ignorance. In your daily life, are you satisfied with knowledge that simply feels true, or do you actively verify it against the truth? What is one area where you need to dig deeper?
?
The term 'thing' in the definition sparked debate about the impossible. Are there any matters of faith you consider 'impossible' and thus dismiss without examination? Could this be a way to avoid challenging your assumptions? Commit to examining one such issue with the intent of seeking truth, even if it is uncomfortable.

Knowledge as Perception and the Faculty of Intellect
Sheikh al-Islam defines knowledge as perception and explains malakah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sheikh al-Islam's definition of knowledge as 'perception of the thing as it is' and the concept of malakah as a settled faculty, along with the three related states of the intellect.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a settled faculty of perception, not a passing impression.

Sheikh al-Islam Zakariya al-Ansari offers a concise definition of knowledge that focuses on perception. He then connects this to the concept of malakah—a settled disposition in the soul—which is the basis for the intellect's different stages. This passage lays out the terminology that later theologians and logicians would refine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the succinctness of the definition and the progression of ideas.
The passage begins with the definition and then explains malakah.
2
Understand the definition of knowledge
The definition is 'perception of the thing as it is.' Consider how it differs from other definitions and why perception is central.
This definition emphasizes direct awareness of reality.
3
Explore the concept of malakah
Malakah is a settled disposition. The commentary explains it as a stable ability in the soul, as if it has taken possession.
The term is related to 'milk' (possession).
4
Distinguish the three intellect states
The passage mentions actual intellect, acquired intellect, and intellect as a faculty (potential). Understand their order and relationship.
These stages represent the development from potential to actualized knowledge.

Commentary
This passage provides a second definition of knowledge from Sheikh al-Islam Zakariya al-Ansari: 'Perception of the thing as it is' (إدراك الشيء على ما هو به). This is slightly different from the earlier definition which used 'according to its reality' (بحقيقته). The passage then states that it is also called a 'malakah' (faculty, الملكة). The commentator explains malakah as a settled disposition in the soul, indicating a stable ability. He further clarifies that this malakah is called 'actual intellect' (عقل بالفعل) or 'acquired intellect' (عقل مستفاد), while the prior preparedness is 'intellect as a faculty' (عقل بالملكة). The passage notes that al-Kastali elaborated on this in his gloss.

The Two Definitions of Knowledge
Al-Ansari gives two definitions of absolute knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of knowledge given by Al-Ansari and why both are needed.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either perception or a faculty—both capture its nature.

In this passage, Al-Ansari presents two complementary definitions of knowledge. The first defines it as perception of reality; the second as a stable faculty. These definitions lay the groundwork for distinguishing true knowledge from mere opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic text and translation for the two definitions of knowledge.
Notice the shift from 'knowledge is' to 'it is said'.
2
Understand the first definition
The first definition: perception of a thing as it is. This is the standard definition in kalam.
This emphasizes correspondence with reality.
3
Understand the second definition
The second definition: a faculty enabling perception of particulars. This highlights knowledge as a power, not just an event.
The term malaka means a stable quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصل كلمة وبعد» وتعريف مطلق العلم والجهل شيخ الإسلام : إدراك الشئ على ما هو به. ويقال: ملكة يقتدر بها على إدراكات جزئية. والجهل : انتفاء العلم بالمقصود؛ بأن لم يدرك؛ وهو الجهل البسيط، أو أدرك على خلاف هيئته في الواقع؛ وهو الجهل المركب؛ لتركبه من جهلين: جهل المدرك بما في الواقع، وجهله بأنه جاهل، كاعتقاد الفلسفي قدم العالم. انتهى.
The origin of the word and after, and the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance. Shaykh al-Islam said: Knowledge is perception of a thing as it is. And it is said: a faculty (malaka) by which one is capable of perceiving particular things. And ignorance: the absence of knowledge concerning the intended object; either it is not perceived—that is simple ignorance—or it is perceived contrary to its actual state—that is compound ignorance, because it is composed of two ignorances: the perceiver's ignorance of what is in reality and his ignorance that he is ignorant, like the philosophers' belief in the pre-eternity of the world. Concluded.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge and ignorance as absolute categories in kalam. It opens with a heading indicating it is the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance. Then it quotes Shaykh al-Islam (referring to the author or a senior authority) giving two definitions of knowledge. The first definition: knowledge is the perception of a thing as it is in reality (idrak al-shay' 'ala ma huwa bihi). This is the standard Ash‘ari definition of knowledge. The second definition, introduced with 'it is said' (yuqalu), states that knowledge is a faculty (malaka) that enables one to perceive particular things (juz’iyyat). This emphasizes that knowledge is not just individual acts of perception but a stable ability. Then the definition of ignorance (al-jahl) follows. Ignorance is the lack of knowledge concerning the object that is intended to be known (al-maqsud). This is an important restriction: it excludes things not meant to be known, such as unknowable matters. The passage distinguishes two types: simple ignorance (jahl basit), which is simply not perceiving the object (the absence of knowledge without false belief); and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which occurs when one perceives something contrary to its actual state. It is called 'compound' because it combines two ignorances: ignorance of the true reality, and ignorance of one's own ignorance. The example given is the philosophers' belief in the pre-eternity of the world (qidam al-‘alam)—they falsely believe the world is eternal, making them ignorant both of its createdness and of their own error. The passage ends with 'concluded' (intaha).

Key Terms
علم
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second definition of knowledge given in this passage?
A) Perception of a thing as it is.
B) A faculty enabling perception of particulars.
C) Absence of ignorance.
D) Belief without justification.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is defined both as accurate perception and as a stable faculty for perceiving particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which definition—perception or faculty—exposes a weakness in how you pursue knowledge? What will you do today to strengthen that side?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Does it meet the test of 'perceiving it as it is'? If not, what might you be avoiding seeing?
?
If knowledge is also a faculty that must be developed, what is one habit you will change this week to train your mind for deeper understanding?

Simple and Compound Ignorance
Al-Ansari defines ignorance and distinguishes its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between simple ignorance and compound ignorance, with an example.

Core Idea
Simple ignorance is not knowing; compound ignorance is knowing falsely.

After defining knowledge, Al-Ansari turns to ignorance. He distinguishes between the mere absence of knowledge (simple ignorance) and the far more dangerous state of having a false belief (compound ignorance). This distinction is crucial for identifying errors in theology.

Definition of Simple and Compound Ignorance
The author defines ignorance and distinguishes two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between simple and compound ignorance and why compound ignorance is considered more problematic.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is worse because you don't even know that you don't know.

Ignorance may seem straightforward, but Islamic scholars distinguish two types. This lesson, drawn from a classical kalam text, defines ignorance and explains why one type is more dangerous. You'll learn the precise definitions and see an example that illustrates the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the two types
Look for the definitions of simple and compound ignorance. Pay attention to the key terms.
The author uses 'جهل بسيط' and 'جهل مركب'.
3
Understand the example
Consider the example of the philosopher's belief. Why is it compound ignorance?
4
Reflect on the danger
Think about why compound ignorance is more problematic than simple ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهل (۱): انتفاء العلم بالمقصود (۲)؛ بأن لم يُدْرَك؛ وهو الجهل البسيط، أو أُدْرِكَ على خلاف هيئته في الواقع (۳)؛ وهو الجهل المركَّب؛ لتركبه من جهلين (٤) : جهلُ المُدرِك بما في الواقع ، وجهله بأنَّه جاهل ، كاعتقاد الفلسفي قدم العالم (٥) . انتهى .
And ignorance (1): absence of knowledge of the intended (2); by not having perceived it; and that is simple ignorance, or having perceived it contrary to its reality (3); and that is compound ignorance; because it is composed of two ignorances (4): the perceiver's ignorance of what is in reality, and his ignorance that he is ignorant, like the philosopher's belief in the pre-eternity of the world (5). End.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of ignorance. It begins by stating that ignorance is the absence of knowledge of what is intended to be known. If the object has not been perceived at all, that is simple ignorance. If it has been perceived but in a way contrary to its actual reality, that is compound ignorance. The term 'compound' refers to its consisting of two ignorances: ignorance of the true state of affairs and ignorance of one's own ignorance. The example given is the philosophers' belief in the pre-eternity of the world, which is a mistaken belief that involves both not knowing the truth and not realizing one's error.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance can be simple (lack of knowledge) or compound (false belief combined with unawareness), and the latter is more dangerous because it prevents learning.

Summary vs. Detailed Evidence: Knowing and Defending Faith
Question-answer definition of two types of evidence with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between summary and detailed evidence in Islamic theology and how this distinction applies to the proof of God's existence.

Core Idea
Knowing is not enough; detailed evidence means you can defend it.

The author addresses a practical question: how do we distinguish between the knowledge required of common believers and that required of scholars? The answer lies in two types of evidence. This passage defines them both and then illustrates with the classic proof of God's existence from the world. Understanding this distinction is key to knowing your own level of theological duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and definitions
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the question sets up the need for a distinction between two levels of evidence.
The question asks about the definitions themselves, not yet about application.
2
Understand the key ability: articulation and defense
Notice that the key difference is the ability to articulate the evidence ('taqrir') and to repel doubts ('daf' al-shubah'). Summary evidence lacks this; detailed evidence has it.
This ability is what separates the knowledge of the common person from that of the elite scholar.
3
Apply the example to see the difference in practice
Work through the example of proving God's existence from the world. See how merely stating the evidence is summary, while explaining the connection and defending against objections makes it detailed.
This concrete example makes the abstract definition clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: فما حد الدليل الإجمالي ، وحد الدليل التفصيلي؟ حتّى أعرِفَ ما يجب على عامة الخلق ، وما يجب على خاصتهم. فالجواب عن ذلك: أنَّ الدَّليل الإجمالي هو الذي يعجز صاحِبه عن تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه الشُّبه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو ما يقدر صاحبه على تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه من الشبه ، من وخذ لك مثلا تتضح منه هذه الحقيقة، إذ قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجود الله تعالى؟ فقلت: الذي يدل على وجود الله تعالى هو هذا العالم، ولم تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجود الله ، أو عرفتها ، ولكنك لم تعرف كيف ترد على ما يُورَدُ على هذا الدليل مِن الشُّبه ، فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل الإجمالي ، وأما إذا كنت تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجوده سبحانه ، وكنت مع ذلك تستطيع أن تدفع كلَّ شُبهة يورِدُها المُورِدُ على هذا الدليل فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل التفصيلي.
If you said: What is the definition of the summary evidence and the definition of the detailed evidence, so that I may know what is obligatory upon the common people and what is obligatory upon their elite? The answer to that: The summary evidence is that which its possessor is unable to articulate and to repel doubts that arise against it. As for the detailed evidence, it is that which its possessor is able to articulate and to repel doubts that arise against it. For example, take an example to clarify this reality: When it is said to you, 'What is the evidence for the existence of Allah, exalted?', and you say, 'That which indicates the existence of Allah, exalted, is this world,' but you do not know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to the existence of Allah, or you know it but you do not know how to repel the doubts that are brought against this evidence, then you are a knower of the summary evidence. But if you know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to His existence, may He be glorified, and with that you are able to repel every doubt that the objector brings against this evidence, then you are a knower of the detailed evidence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer definition. The author poses a question: what is the definition of summary and detailed evidence, so that one can distinguish the obligation of the common people from that of the elite. The answer: Summary evidence is that which a person knows but cannot articulate clearly nor defend against doubts. Detailed evidence is that which a person can explain and defend against all doubts. The author then provides a concrete example using the proof of God's existence from the world. If someone merely says 'the world indicates God' but cannot explain the reasoning or respond to objections, that person has summary evidence. If they can explain the logical connection and rebut every doubt, they have detailed evidence. This clarifies the practical distinction between the two types of evidence.

Key Terms
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي
تقرير
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think summary evidence is insufficient for belief or that detailed evidence is required for all believers.
Correction: The passage shows that summary evidence is sufficient for common believers; detailed evidence is required only for the elite who must articulate and defend the faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between summary and detailed evidence?
A) Summary evidence is false, detailed is true
B) Detailed evidence is longer than summary
C) Detailed evidence allows one to articulate and defend against doubts
D) Summary evidence is for scholars only

Key Takeaway
Summary evidence is sufficient for common belief, but detailed evidence includes the ability to explain and defend that belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content with merely knowing that God exists, or have you taken the time to understand the reasoning behind it so you can defend it when challenged? What would it take for you to move from summary to detailed evidence in your own faith?
?
When someone questions your belief in God, do you find yourself unable to articulate your reasoning? Does that make you feel insecure? What step can you take this week to strengthen your understanding of the evidence?
?
The passage distinguishes between the common people and the elite. Do you consider yourself among the elite? If not, are you willing to put in the effort to become someone who can articulate and defend their faith?

The Obligation of Learning and Teaching Tawhid
The commentator defines the obligation of learning and teaching tawhid with Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why learning and teaching tawhid is an absolute obligation in Islam, based on the Quranic command.

Core Idea
Learning and teaching tawhid is a non-negotiable duty commanded by Allah.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes the foundational obligation of learning and teaching the principles of Islamic theology. The commentator clarifies that this duty is absolute, with no concession, and supports it with the Quranic verse 'So know that there is no god but Allah'. Let's examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key phrase 'واجب محتما' (absolute obligation).
Pay attention to the parenthetical numbers which correspond to the commentary.
2
Identify the proof
Notice the Quranic verse quoted as evidence. Reflect on why knowing Allah is presented as a command.
The verse is from Surah Muhammad.
3
Understand the nature of the obligation
Contrast the phrase 'لا ترخيص فيه' (no concession) with other religious duties. Why is this duty so emphatic?
Tawhid is the foundation of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني كم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية (١): أنَّ تعلُّم التّوحيد وتعليمه واجب (۲) شرعاً وجوبًا محتما، أي لا ترخيص فيه (۳) ؛ لقوله تعالى (٤): ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد: ١٩] = وقيل: هو كافر، وعليه جرى السنوسي في شرح الكبرى ونصره، وشنّع على من قال: إن التقليد كافٍ ، ويقال : إنه رجع إلى القول بكفاية التقليد، والذي تطمئن إليه النَّفس هو القول الأول من هذه الأقوال ؛ لأنَّ مِن العوام مَن لا يستطيع فهم هذه الأدلة ، ولأنَّ الرَّسول كان يقبل من كلِّ واحدٍ ما قدر عليه. ولم يُعرف عنه التشدد في حمل الناس على إدراك ما لم يمنحهم الله القدرة على إدراكه ، وسيأتي لهذا الكلام تتمة عند إثارة هذه المسألة في كلام المصنف.
Meaning: how much you learn the principles of religion with summary and detailed evidences (1): that learning and teaching tawhid is obligatory (2) by law, an absolute obligation, i.e. no concession in it (3); due to His saying (4): {So know that there is no god but Allah} [Muhammad: 19] = And it is said: he is a disbeliever, and upon that al-Sanusi proceeded in his Sharh al-Kubra and supported it, and he denounced those who said that taqlid is sufficient. And it is said: he retracted to the saying of sufficiency of taqlid. And that which the soul is at ease with is the first of these sayings, because among the common people there are those who cannot understand these evidences, and because the Messenger used to accept from each person what he was capable of. And it is not known that he was strict in forcing people to perceive what Allah did not grant them the ability to perceive. And there will come a completion of this speech when raising this issue in the words of the author.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the obligation of learning and teaching tawhid. It begins by stating the meaning of the line from the poem: "كم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية" and then clarifies that learning and teaching tawhid is an absolute obligation with no concession. The proof is the verse "فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله". Then the commentator discusses a disagreement: some say the one who does not learn is a disbeliever, and al-Sanusi held this view, but the commentator favors the view that taqlid is sufficient for common people, citing the Prophet's leniency. The passage ends with a note that this will be completed later.

Key Terms
تعلُّم التّوحيد
تعليمه
واجب محتما
لا ترخيص فيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think learning tawhid is only recommended, not obligatory.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says it is an absolute obligation with no concession.

Checkpoint
What is the status of learning and teaching tawhid according to this passage?
A) Recommended
B) Absolute obligation
C) Permissible
D) Optional

Key Takeaway
Learning and teaching tawhid is an absolute religious duty, proven by the Quranic command to know Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that learning tawhid is an absolute obligation, not a recommendation. Do you truly know Allah as He deserves, or have you been satisfied with a superficial belief? What specific knowledge will you seek starting this week?
?
The command to teach tawhid is binding on you. Are you actively teaching your family or friends about the oneness of Allah, or have you neglected this duty? Whom can you teach before next Friday?
?
If there is no concession in this obligation, what excuses have you been making for not learning or teaching tawhid? Will you repent and begin today?

The Debate on Taqlid: Is It Sufficient for Common People?
The commentator presents the debate on taqlid in tawhid and states his preferred view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement about whether common people can rely on taqlid for tawhid, and the commentator's preferred view.

Core Idea
Taqlid may be sufficient for those unable to understand detailed proofs.

After establishing the obligation of learning tawhid, the commentary addresses a key debate: must every individual master detailed theological proofs, or is it acceptable for common people to rely on taqlid (imitation)? The commentator presents different opinions and gives his preferred view based on mercy and the Prophet's example.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic text about the disagreement on taqlid. Note the different positions mentioned.
Look for the phrase 'وقيل: هو كافر' and the subsequent views.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three positions: (1) the one who doesn't learn is a disbeliever, (2) taqlid is sufficient, (3) al-Sanusi's view and his retraction.
The commentator mentions that al-Sanusi initially held the strict view.
3
Analyze the commentator's preference
Note that the commentator favors the view that taqlid is sufficient for common people, giving two reasons: inability to understand and the Prophet's leniency.
This reflects a practical and merciful approach.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate balances the obligation of knowledge with the capacity of individuals.
This is a classic tension in Islamic theology.

And there will come a completion of this speech when raising this issue in the words of the author.

?

Individual and Communal Obligations in Kalam
Definitions of two obligations and types of evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the individual and communal obligations in studying kalam, the minimum evidence required for each, and the meaning of 'strength' in arguments.

Core Idea
Knowing kalam is both a personal duty and a communal responsibility.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid defines two categories of obligation in studying theology: individual ('ayni) and communal (kifa'i). It clarifies the minimum evidence needed—summary for the individual, detailed for the community—and emphasizes that communal obligation requires the ability to refute doubts with arguments that follow logical rules. The footnotes further explain the nature of detailed and summary evidence and what 'strength' means in debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the main definitions, then footnotes elaborating key terms.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two obligations
Find the definitions of 'ayni and kifa'i. Note their differences in scope and minimum requirement.
The individual obligation requires leaving taqlid; the communal requires enabling verification.
3
Understand evidence types
From the footnotes, grasp the distinction between detailed (dafili tafsili) and summary (ijmali) evidence. Ask: Who needs which?
Detailed evidence is fully analyzable; summary is not.
4
Grasp 'with strength'
Study the explanation of 'bi-quwwa' and the example. Understand that strength means following logical principles the opponent cannot reject.
The example shows the difference between sound and unsound refutations.
5
Reflect on personal duty
Consider your own obligation: Do you have at least summary evidence for your core beliefs? What would it take to move to detailed evidence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عينيا (١) في العيني منه ، وهو ما يخرج به المكلف مِن التَّقليد إلى التحقيق وأقله : معرفة كلّ عقيدة بدليل ولو جُمْليًّا. وكفائيا في الكفائي منه ، وهو: ما يُقتدر معه على تحقيق مسائله ، وإقامة الأدلة التفصيليَّة (٢) عليها ، وإزالة الشُّبه عنها بقوة (۳) . (۱) قوله : ( عينيا) نسبة إلى العين بمعنى الذات ؛ لتعلقه بعين كل شخص على حدته ، ثم هو وجوب فروع على صحة إيمان المقلد ، ووجوب أصول على كفره ، ويأتي تفصيل ذلك. الأمير. (۲) قوله: (الأدلة التفصيلية الدليل التفصيلي: هو المقدور على تقريره وحلّ شُبهه، والإجمالي : هو المعجوز عنهما أو عن أحدهما . الشنواني. (۳) قوله: (بقوة ) راجع لما تقدم من قوله تحقيق مسائله وإقامة الأدلة التفصيلية عليها وإزالة الشبه عنها، ومعنى كونها بقوة أن يكون جاريا على القوانين التي لا يقدح الخصم فيها ، مثلا إذا قال الخصم : الحركة مستترة ، فيرد الجاري على القواعد أنه يلزم على الاستتار الجمع بين الضدين، فلو قلنا: يلزم الجمع بين الخلافين لم يكن جاريا على القواعد ؛ لأن الخلافين يجتمعان . الشنواني .
Individual (1) in the individual obligation, and it is that by which the person responsible departs from taqlid to verification, and its minimum is knowledge of every creed with evidence, even if summary. And communal in the communal obligation, which is that by which one is able to verify its issues, and establish detailed evidences (2) upon them, and remove doubts from them with strength (3). (1) His saying: ('ayniyya) is a relation to 'ayn meaning essence, because it pertains to the very person individually. Then it is an obligation of branches for the validity of the faith of the muqallid, and an obligation of fundamentals for his disbelief. Its detail will come. Al-Amir. (2) His saying: (the detailed evidences) the detailed evidence is that which one is capable of establishing and resolving its doubts, and the summary is that which one is incapable of both or one of them. Al-Shinwani. (3) His saying: (with strength) refers to what preceded of his saying: verifying its issues, establishing detailed evidences upon them, and removing doubts from them. The meaning of it being with strength is that it runs according to the principles that the opponent cannot attack. For example, if the opponent says: motion is hidden, the response according to the rules is that hiddenness necessitates the conjunction of opposites. If we said: it necessitates the conjunction of differences, it would not run according to the principles, because differences can coexist. Al-Shinwani.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the individual ('ayni) and communal (kifa'i) obligations regarding learning kalam. The individual obligation is to leave taqlid and achieve verification, with a minimum of knowing each creed with at least a summary evidence. The communal obligation is the ability to verify the issues, establish detailed evidences, and refute doubts with strength. Then the footnotes explain: 'ayni relates to each person individually, and its ruling differs for branches and fundamentals. The detailed evidence is one that one can present and resolve its doubts, while summary evidence is one that one cannot do either. Lastly, "with strength" means the evidence must follow logical rules that the opponent cannot refute, giving an example about the fallacy of hiddenness.

Key Terms
عينيا
كفائيا
التقليد
التحقيق
الدليل التفصيلي
الدليل الإجمالي
بقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the individual obligation requires detailed evidence for every creed.
Correction: The text explicitly states the minimum is knowledge with summary evidence, not detailed.

Checkpoint
What is the minimum for the individual obligation in learning kalam?
A) Mastering detailed evidences
B) Knowing each creed with at least summary evidence
C) Memorizing the texts
D) Following a scholar

Key Takeaway
Every Muslim must personally know the basics of faith with at least summary proof, while the community must ensure some scholars can defend the religion with detailed, logically strong arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you personally satisfied with taqlid in your foundational beliefs, or have you sought tahqiq? What is the minimum evidence you possess for each creed, and is it sufficient according to this lesson?
?
If the communal obligation of kalam requires some to master detailed evidence and remove doubts, are you contributing to this duty or leaving it entirely to others? What is your specific responsibility given your knowledge and circumstances?
?
The passage teaches that arguments must be 'with strength' – according to logical principles. When you defend your faith, do you ensure your reasoning is sound and irrefutable, or do you rely on emotional appeals that an opponent could easily dismiss? How can you strengthen your arguments today?

Defining the Subject of Ilm al-Kalam
The commentator enumerates and evaluates definitions of the subject of ilm al-kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the favored definition of the subject matter of ilm al-kalam according to the commentator, and how it compares to alternative definitions.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is Allah's essence, attributes, and possible beings as they relate to Him.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a foundational question: what exactly does ilm al-kalam study? The commentator presents several competing definitions found in the tradition, then indicates which one is strongest. By examining these definitions, we gain clarity on the scope and purpose of this science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the discussion.
The passage cites different scholarly opinions on the subject of kalam.
2
Identify the preferred definition
Note that al-Amir explicitly states his preference: the subject is Allah's essence and attributes and possible beings from the perspective of their origin.
This is presented as 'more apparent' than other views.
3
Compare with other definitions
List the other definitions mentioned: the three judgments, absolute existents, the known absolutely, etc. Understand why they are considered less comprehensive.
The commentator dismisses them as not strong.
4
Examine Shaykh al-Islam's definition
Note the additional detail: the subject includes the essence of Allah from the three rational categories (necessary, impossible, permissible) and similarly for messengers, and the possible as a means to prove the Creator.
This expands on the initial definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ موضوع علم الكلام وتعريفه] وهذا العلم يُبحث فيه عن محمد رسول الله على ما يأتي، فلعل الشارح اقتصر على الأشرف، ولغيره دليل آخر نحو عَامِنُواْ بِمَا نَزَلْنَا [النساء: ٤٧] فإنه يشمل الكل أو بالقياس أو غير ذلك . الأمير. (٤) هذا الكلام يشير إلى موضوع علم أصول الدين ، أو علم التّوحيد، أو علم الكلام، وهو يفيد أنَّ موضوعه : ذاتُ الله تعالى وصفاته، والممكنات من حيث مبدؤُها، لأنه يُبحث فيه عن ذلك، قال العلامة الأمير: «وهو أظهر مما قيل: موضوعه المعلوم مطلقا، أو ماهيات دلالتها على ما يجب للإله كما في شرح الكبرى»، أو أقسام الحكم الثلاثة - وهي الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز ـ أو مطلق الموجودات ؛ إلى غير ذلك من أقوال لا تقوى. انتهى كلامه . الممكنات العقلي من حيث وقال شيخ الإسلام وموضوعه ذات الله تعالى من حيث ما يجب له، وما يستحيل، وما يجوز ، وذات الرُّسل الكرام كذلك ، والممكن من حيث إنَّه يتوصل به إلى وجود صانعه ، =
[The subject matter of ilm al-kalam and its definition] And this discipline is investigated in it about Muhammad, the Messenger of God, as will come. Perhaps the commentator restricted himself to the most honorable, and for others there is another evidence like 'Believe in what we have sent down' [al-Baqara 4:47] for it encompasses all, or by analogy, or other. Al-Amir. (4) This speech refers to the subject matter of the science of the principles of religion, or the science of tawhid, or ilm al-kalam, and it indicates that its subject is the essence of God Most High and His attributes, and possible beings from the perspective of their origin, because it investigates about that. The scholar al-Amir said: 'And it is more apparent than what was said: its subject is the known absolutely, or the essences whose indication is what is necessary for the divine as in Sharh al-Kubra,' or the three judgments - which are obligation, impossibility, and permissibility - or absolute existents; to other sayings that are not strong. End of his speech. The rational possible beings from the perspective. And Shaykh al-Islam said: its subject is the essence of God Most High from the perspective of what is necessary for Him, what is impossible, and what is permissible, and the essence of the noble messengers likewise, and the possible from the perspective that it reaches to the existence of its Maker.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of ilm al-kalam. It begins by noting that the discipline investigates matters including the prophethood of Muhammad, but the commentator may have restricted to the most honorable. Then it cites the view that the subject is Allah's essence and attributes and possible beings from their origin. Al-Amir prefers this over other definitions such as absolute known, or essences indicating what is necessary for God, or the three judgments, or absolute existents. Then it mentions Shaykh al-Islam's definition: the essence of Allah from the necessary, impossible, and permissible, and similarly the messengers, and the possible as a means to prove the existence of the Creator.

Key Terms
موضوع علم الكلام
ذات الله
صفاته
الممكنات
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the subject of ilm al-kalam is merely the three rational judgments (necessity, impossibility, permissibility).
Correction: While these judgments are central tools, the commentator holds that the proper subject is broader: Allah's essence and attributes and possible beings. The judgments are not the subject themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the preferred definition of the subject of ilm al-kalam?
A) The three rational judgments
B) Absolute existents
C) Allah's essence and attributes and possible beings
D) The known absolutely

Key Takeaway
The subject of ilm al-kalam is Allah's essence, His attributes, and possible beings considered in relation to their origin from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the subject of ilm al-kalam, do you tend to focus only on proving God's existence, or do you also reflect deeply on His essence and attributes? Which part of the definition challenges you to grow in your understanding of Allah?
?
The commentator favors a definition that includes possible beings as a means to know the Creator. How often do you look at the world around you as evidence leading you to the existence and attributes of Allah? What one thing in your life right now could serve as a witness to His perfection?
?
If the subject of kalam is misdefined (e.g., only as the three judgments), what might you miss about the reality of Allah? Could a limited definition lead to a limited worship? Examine your own heart: do you relate to Allah only through what is 'necessary' for Him, or do you also contemplate His attributes and His creation as pathways to deeper love?

Personal and Sufficiency Obligations in Kalam
Defines the personal and sufficiency obligations in learning kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the personal and sufficiency obligations in learning kalam and what each requires.

Core Idea
Belief must be verified, not inherited; defending it is a communal duty.

Sanusi distinguishes two levels of duty in acquiring theological knowledge. The first is a personal obligation on every Muslim to know the basic creeds with evidence, freeing oneself from blind imitation. The second is a communal duty to master the science of kalam for defending the faith. This short passage defines both clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own definitions come through.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two types of obligation. Find each one and note the key terms: 'ainī and kifā'ī.
Look for the words 'العيني' and 'الكفائي'.
3
Understand the minimum requirement for each
For the personal obligation, note the minimum: 'معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو جملة'. For the sufficiency obligation, note what it enables: 'تحقيق مسائله، وإقامة الأدلة التفصيلية، وإزالة الشبه'.
The personal duty is basic; the sufficiency duty is detailed.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why is a simple proof enough for personal belief, but detailed proof required for community defense? How does this balance individual responsibility and communal scholarship?
Consider the phrase 'ولو جملة' (even if summarily).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عينيا في العيني منه ، وهو ما يخرج به المكلف من التقليد إلى التحقيق وأقله : معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو جملة. وكفائيا في الكفائي منه ، وهو: ما يقتدر معه على تحقيق مسائله ، وإقامة الأدلة التفصيلية عليها ، وإزالة الشبه عنها بقوة.
Personal obligation in that which is personal: it is that by which the responsible person emerges from taqlīd (imitation) to taḥqīq (verification); its minimum is knowledge of every creed with a proof, even if summarily. And sufficiency obligation in that which is sufficiency: it is that by which one is able to investigate its issues, establish detailed proofs for them, and remove doubts from them with strength.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines two categories of obligation in learning the science of kalam. First, the 'ainī (personal) obligation: this refers to what every individual must acquire to move from blind taqlīd (imitation) to actual taḥqīq (verification) of their beliefs. The minimum requirement is knowing each creed (such as belief in God, prophethood, etc.) along with a proof, even if the proof is only general or summary (jummali). Second, the kifā'ī (sufficiency) obligation: this is a communal duty; it is the level of knowledge that enables the person to investigate the detailed issues of kalam, formulate elaborate proofs, and successfully refute doubts. The phrase 'with strength' (bi-quwwa) means the arguments must follow sound logical rules that an opponent cannot undermine. Thus, the passage distinguishes between the basic personal duty to be a convinced believer and the advanced communal duty to be a defender of the faith.

Key Terms
العيني (al-'ainī)
الكفائي (al-kifā'ī)
التقليد (taqlīd)
التحقيق (taḥqīq)
الدليل (dalīl)
جملة (jummali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the personal obligation requires detailed proofs for every creed.
Correction: The text explicitly says the minimum is 'معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو جملة' — even a general proof suffices.

Checkpoint
What is the minimum requirement for the personal obligation in learning kalam?
A) Knowing each creed with a detailed proof.
B) Knowing each creed with a proof, even if general.
C) Being able to establish detailed proofs for all issues.
D) Removing doubts from others.

Key Takeaway
The personal obligation in kalam requires every believer to have at least a summary proof for each creed, while the sufficiency obligation requires scholars to provide detailed proofs and defend the faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you still relying on the faith of your parents without having even a summary proof for your belief in Allah? What would it take for you to move from taqlid to tahqiq—and when will you start?
?
If you feel secure in your personal verification, ask yourself: who in your community is carrying the sufficiency obligation to defend the faith? Is it possible that you are neglecting this communal duty while benefiting from others' efforts?
?
Do you truly care about the doubts that people around you have about Islam? If you were the only one able to remove those doubts, would you have the knowledge and strength to do so—or would you let them fall away?

The Comprehensive Subject of Kalam
States the subject matter of kalam as God, His attributes, and contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that kalam's subject matter includes not only God's essence and attributes but also the states of contingent beings from their origin (al-mabda') to their return (al-ma'ād), all investigated according to the law of Islam.

Core Idea
Kalam studies both the Creator and creation from beginning to end, guided by Islamic law.

This lesson introduces the formal definition of kalam’s subject matter. The author concisely states what this science investigates. As you read, note that the subject matter is broader than just God—it encompasses the entire created realm from its origin to its final return, all under the rule of Islamic principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the literal English translation. Focus on the key terms.
The passage is a single but dense sentence.
2
Identify the three components
The passage lists three areas of investigation: God's essence, God's attributes, and the states of contingent beings. Note that contingent beings are studied in two temporal contexts: the beginning (creation) and the return (afterlife).
Contingent beings are everything that can exist or not exist.
3
Understand the governing principle
The phrase 'according to the law of Islam' (قانون الإسلام) sets the methodological boundary. This means kalam's inquiries are guided by the Qur'an, Sunna, consensus, and sound reason aligned with them, distinguishing it from purely philosophical speculation.
This ensures kalam remains within Islamic orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا العلم يبحث فيه عن ذات الله وصفاته ، وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد، على قانون الإسلام.
And this science investigates concerning the essence of God and His attributes, and the states of contingent beings in the beginning and the return, according to the law of Islam.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter (mawḍū') of kalam. It states that the science investigates three main areas: first, the essence of God (dhāt Allāh) — for example, that He is eternal, not like created things; second, His attributes (ṣifāt) — such as power, knowledge, will; and third, the states (aḥwāl) of contingent beings (al-mumkināt), meaning everything that can exist or not exist, specifically regarding their origin (al-mabda') — that they are created by God's choice, not by necessity — and their return (al-ma'ād) — the afterlife, resurrection, judgment. The phrase 'according to the law of Islam' (qānūn al-Islām) indicates that the investigation is guided by the principles of the Qur'an, Sunna, consensus, and sound reason that does not contradict them. This distinguishes Islamic kalam from Greek philosophy, which uses purely rational speculation. Thus, the subject is comprehensive: it covers both the Creator and the creation from an Islamic perspective.

Key Terms
ذات الله (dhāt Allāh)
صفات (ṣifāt)
الممكنات (al-mumkināt)
المبدأ (al-mabda')
المعاد (al-ma'ād)
قانون الإسلام (qānūn al-Islām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam only discusses God, but it also includes creation's states (beginning and afterlife) within an Islamic framework.
Correction: The text explicitly includes 'أحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد' – the states of contingent beings in the beginning and the return – showing the subject is comprehensive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the subject matter of kalam include?
A) Only the essence and attributes of God.
B) The essence and attributes of God, and the states of contingent beings in the beginning and return.
C) Only the possible and impossible judgments.
D) Only the five pillars of Islam.

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of kalam encompasses God's essence and attributes, and the states of contingent beings from their origin to their final return, all investigated according to the law of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a contingent being. Does your daily life reflect absolute dependence on God, or do you often feel self-sufficient? What one habit can you change tonight to acknowledge your need for Him?
?
The passage says kalam investigates the 'return' (al-ma'ād). Are you living today as though you will return to your Creator? If not, what concrete step can you take before you sleep to prepare for that meeting?

What Is Kalam?

Kalam as a Science of Proof
Provides a second definition of kalam as a science enabling proof of religious beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of kalam as a science that enables the proof of religious beliefs, including both rational and revealed matters.

Core Idea
Kalam is a tool to prove beliefs, not just a theory.

In the previous lesson, we saw one definition of kalam. Here, al-Sanusi provides another definition that emphasizes the practical outcome: the ability to establish religious beliefs. This definition broadens the scope to include both rational doctrines and matters known only through revelation (sam'iyyat).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the definition's structure become clear.
The pronoun 'وحدوه' refers to earlier scholars.
2
Identify the two categories of beliefs
The definition mentions two types of beliefs: religious beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya) and sam'iyyāt. Notice how each is defined and why both are included.
Sam'iyyāt are matters known only through revelation, like details of the afterlife.
3
Reflect on 'from the perspective of believing them'
Think about why the phrase 'min ḥaythu iʿtiqādihā' is important. It focuses on how to hold these beliefs with evidence, not on their inner nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدوه أيضا بأنه : علم يقتدر معه على إثبات العقائد الدينية والسمعيات من حيث اعتقادها.
And they also defined it as: a science by which one is able to establish the religious beliefs and the sam'iyyāt (matters known through revelation) from the perspective of believing them.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage gives another definition of kalam. It describes it as a science (ʿilm) that enables its possessor to 'establish' (ithbāt) two categories: first, the religious beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya), which are the core doctrines of Islam such as belief in God, angels, books, and messengers; second, the sam'iyyāt (literally 'things heard'), which are matters known solely through revelation, like the details of the afterlife, punishment of the grave, and other eschatological realities. The phrase 'from the perspective of believing them' (min ḥaythu iʿtiqādihā) indicates that the focus is on how to hold these beliefs correctly with evidence, not on their inner nature. This definition emphasizes that kalam is a practical tool for acquiring certainty and defending creed, not just theoretical speculation. The ability (iqtidār) it provides is a communal duty, and it distinguishes the practitioner from the common believer who may lack detailed argumentation.

Key Terms
العقائد الدينية (al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya)
السمعيات (al-sam'iyyāt)
إثبات (ithbāt)
اعتقاد (iʿtiqād)
يقتدر (yaqtadir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam only deals with rational proofs and ignores revelation.
Correction: This definition explicitly includes sam'iyyāt (revelation-based matters), showing that kalam covers both rational and revealed doctrines.

Checkpoint
What does the definition of kalam as 'a science by which one is able to establish religious beliefs and sam'iyyat' emphasize?
A) The necessity of memorizing scripture.
B) The ability to prove and defend beliefs using evidence, including revealed matters.
C) The obligation to argue with everyone.
D) The rejection of reason.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is defined as a science that enables the proof of both rational and revealed beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson says kalam is a science that empowers you to prove even the unseen matters (sam'iyyat). In which area of your faith—such as the afterlife, the punishment of the grave, or the details of paradise—do you feel most vulnerable to doubt, and what will you do to secure that belief with evidence?
?
If you truly believed that kalam gives you the ability to establish your faith, would you continue to neglect its study? What is the real reason you have not dedicated time to learning the proofs of your religion?

What Is Kalam?

The Subject of Kalam: Essence, Attributes, and Contingent Beings
The author defines the subject of kalam as three areas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three components of the subject of kalam: the essence of Allah, His attributes, and the states of contingent beings from origin to return.

Core Idea
Kalam studies God, His attributes, and all contingent beings from creation to afterlife.

Before diving into theological arguments, one must know what kalam is about. The author begins by defining the subject matter: what does this science investigate? This lesson unpacks the first definition, which identifies three core areas of inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage once to see the three components listed. Notice the numbers in parentheses refer to footnotes in the original.
Focus on the nouns: ذات الله, صفاته, أحوال الممكنات.
2
Identify the three components
List the three parts of the subject: (1) essence of Allah, (2) His attributes, (3) states of contingent beings in origin and return.
These correspond to divine essence, divine attributes, and theology of creation and eschatology.
3
Understand the limiting clause
The phrase 'according to the law of Islam' means these inquiries are conducted using Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and sound reason, distinguishing Islamic kalam from philosophy.
This is a crucial methodological boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع علم الكلام وتعريفه
ذات الله (١) وصفاته (٢) ، وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد(٣)، على قانون الإسلام (٤) .
وحدوه أيضا بأنَّه : عِلمٌ يُقتدر (٥) معه على إثبات العقائد الدينيَّة (٦)
والسمعيات من حيث اعتقادها . انتهى كلامه .
(١) قوله : ( عن ذات الله ) أي من حيث إنها قديمة وباقية ومخالفة للحوادث ... الخ .
(٢) قوله: (وصفاته) أي: من حيث تقسيمها لنفسي وسلبي، ومعان ومعنوية، ومتعلقة وغير متعلقة، والمتعلق لعام التعلق ،وخاصه وقديمه وحادثه، كما في صفات الأفعال عند الأشعري إلى غير ذلك ، فهذا غير البحث عن الذات من حيث مجرد ثبوت الصفات المذكورة أولاً ، فلا تكرار الأمير.
(٣) قوله: (في المبدأ أي من حيث إنها حادثة ناشئة بالاختيار لا بالتعليل. وقوله: (والمعاد) إشارة للحشر والسمعيات ، بقيت النبوات ؛ فإما أنه أدرجها في أحوال الممكنات خصوصا، والمعاد إنما يُعلم من الرسول فاستتبع أحكام الرُّسل ، أو أنها أدرجها في الصفات من حيثُ إن الإرسال صفات الأفعال، وإنما يتعلق بمن ثبتت له تلك الأحكام، وأما نحو مبحث من نصب الإمام وتقليد الأئمة ؛ فإنما ذُكر في بعض كتب هذا الفن لكثرة ضلال الفرق الزائغة فيه، وأما قول المصنف : (وكن كما كان خيار الخلق) ونحوه فآداب ذكرها تتميما للفائدة. الأمير.
(٤) قوله: (على قانون الإسلام) أي أصوله من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والمعقول الذي لا يخالفها، وهو متعلق بقوله : ( يبحث) أي: يُبحث فيه عن ذات الله ... إلخ بحثا جاريا على قانون الإسلام، خرج إلهيات الفلسفة فإنها على مجرد ما خيلته لهم آراؤهم، والمراد بأهل الإسلام ما يشمل المعتزلة فكلامهم يعد من علم التوحيد الشنواني.
(٥) قوله : ( يقتدر) إشارة إلى أنه ليس بلازم إلزام الغير بالفعل، بل هو من أشرف المناصب أن نقله الشعراني في اليواقيت والجواهر» أوائله عن ابن عربي من مطلقا، ولا يغتر بم علم الكلام: «مجاهدة مع غير عدو» فإنه لو تُرِكَ التّمرن فيه قبل الحاجة لتعسّر عند الحاجة إليه أو تعذر ، وهكذا الشأن في الأمور الظاهرية فضلا عن الأمور الباطنية ، وإنما هذه جذبة حالية . الأمير.
(٦) قوله : (الدينية) المنسوبة إلى دين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، صوابا كان أو خطأ، فإن الخصم كالمعتزلة مثلا وإن خطأناه في اعتقاده وما يتمسك به في إثباته .. لا يخرجه من علماء علم الكلام، ولا يخرجُ عِلمه الذي يقتدر معه على إثبات عقائده الباطلة من علم الكلام، ذكره السيد . الشنواني .
The subject of the science of kalam and its definition: The essence of Allah (1) and His attributes (2), and the states of contingent beings in their origin and return (3), according to the law of Islam (4). And they also defined it as: a science by which one is enabled (5) to establish religious beliefs (6) and matters heard (al-samʿiyyāt) from the aspect of believing them. His speech ended. (1) His saying: 'concerning the essence of Allah' means from the aspect that it is eternal, everlasting, and dissimilar to contingent things... etc. (2) His saying: 'and His attributes' means from the aspect of their division into self-related (nafsī) and negative (salbī), and meanings (maʿānī) and meaning-related (maʿnawiyya), and related (mutaʿalliqa) and unrelated, and the related is of general relation and specific, and eternal and temporal, as in the attributes of actions according to al-Ashʿarī, etc. So this is other than the inquiry into the essence from the aspect of merely establishing the mentioned attributes first, so there is no repetition. al-Amir. (3) His saying: 'in the origin' means from the aspect that they are originated, arising by choice not by natural causation. And his saying: 'and the return' is a reference to the resurrection and the matters heard (al-samʿiyyāt). The prophecies remain; either he included them in the states of contingent beings specifically, and the return is only known from the messenger so it entails the rulings of the messengers, or he included them in the attributes from the aspect that sending messengers is an attribute of action, and it only concerns one for whom those rulings are established. As for topics like the appointment of an imam and following the imams, they are only mentioned in some books of this art due to the great misguidance of the deviant sects therein. As for the author's saying: 'and be as the best of creation were' and similar, they are etiquettes mentioned to complete the benefit. al-Amir. (4) His saying: 'according to the law of Islam' means its foundations: the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and sound reason that does not contradict them. It is related to his saying: 'one inquires' i.e., inquiry is made in it concerning the essence of Allah, etc., an inquiry proceeding according to the law of Islam. It excludes the metaphysics of philosophy, for it is based on mere imaginations concocted by their opinions. By 'people of Islam' is meant what includes the Muʿtazila, for their discourse is counted as part of the science of tawḥīd. al-Shanawānī. (5) His saying: 'by which one is enabled' indicates that it is not necessary to impose it on others actively, rather it is among the noblest positions. He quoted al-Shaʿrānī in 'al-Yawāqīt wa al-Jawāhir' from Ibn ʿArabī absolutely, and let no one be deceived by the saying about kalam: 'a struggle against a non-enemy', for if one omits practicing it before need, it becomes difficult or impossible when needed, and such is the case in external matters, let alone internal matters. But this is a momentary ecstatic impulse. al-Amir. (6) His saying: 'religious beliefs' attributed to the religion of Muḥammad, peace be upon him, whether correct or mistaken. For the opponent like the Muʿtazila, even if we consider them mistaken in their belief and what they hold to prove it... does not exclude them from the scholars of kalam, nor does their knowledge by which they are enabled to establish their false beliefs become excluded from the science of kalam. Mentioned by al-Sayyid. al-Shanawānī.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of kalam and provides a second definition of the discipline itself. It begins by stating that kalam investigates the essence of Allah and His attributes, and the states of contingent beings from their origin (al-mabdaʾ) to their return (al-maʿād), all according to the law of Islam. Then it gives another definition from others: kalam is a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya) and the samʿiyyāt (matters known only from revelation) from the aspect of belief in them. The commentary then explains each part: (1) The essence of Allah is studied in terms of being eternal, everlasting, and dissimilar to creation. (2) The attributes are studied in their various divisions—self-related, negative, meanings, meaning-related, related or unrelated, general or specific, eternal or temporal—avoiding repetition with the essence. (3) The origin (al-mabdaʾ) means contingent beings are originated by choice, not by natural necessity; the return (al-maʿād) refers to resurrection and samʿiyyāt; the prophecies are included either under contingent beings or under attributes of actions. (4) 'Law of Islam' means the sources: Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and sound reason, distinguishing Islamic kalam from pagan metaphysics. (5) 'Yaqtadir' indicates the practical benefit of kalam: enabling one to defend beliefs, not imposing them; practicing kalam is necessary to avoid difficulty when needed. (6) 'Religious' includes even mistaken beliefs (like those of the Muʿtazila) because the science encompasses their methods.

Key Terms
ذات الله
صفات الله
أحوال الممكنات
المبدأ
المعاد
قانون الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kalam only studies God's essence and not attributes, or that it does not include the study of contingent beings and the afterlife.
Correction: The definition explicitly includes all three: essence, attributes, and contingent beings from origin to return.

Checkpoint
What are the three components of the subject of kalam according to the first definition?
A) Only the essence of Allah
B) The essence and attributes of Allah
C) The essence and attributes of Allah, and the states of contingent beings in origin and return
D) Only the proofs of religious beliefs

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam encompasses three fundamental areas: Allah's essence, His attributes, and the states of all contingent beings from origin to afterlife, all studied within the framework of Islamic law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage defines kalam's subject as including 'the states of contingent beings in their origin and return.' Where do you stand in your own origin and return? Are you actively preparing for the return, or have you allowed the distractions of this life to make you forget your end?
?
The phrase 'according to the law of Islam' reminds us that theology must be rooted in revelation. When you learn about Allah's essence and attributes, do you approach it with the humility of one submitting to divine law, or do you treat it as a mere intellectual exercise?
?
You have just read that kalam studies 'the essence of Allah and His attributes.' Have you ever truly pondered the greatness of the One whose essence is beyond your comprehension, or have you been content with shallow knowledge? What will you do today to deepen your awe of Him?

What Is Kalam?
