Linguistic Analysis of al-Wahdaniyya
Linguistic analysis of wahdaniyya with morphological breakdown and scholarly debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the morphological structure of the word 'al-wahdaniyya' and the scholarly debate about its derivation.

Core Idea
Al-wahdaniyya is a relative noun with intensification, not an abstract infinitive.

Before exploring the theological significance of tawhid, we must understand the precise meaning of the term 'al-wahdaniyya'. The author begins by analyzing its linguistic form, explaining how the word is constructed from 'al-wahda' (oneness) and addressing a disagreement among scholars about its derivation. This ground-clearing ensures we grasp the depth of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Carefully read the first part of the passage (up to 'li-qasd al-mubalagha') along with the literal translation. Notice the technical terms nisba, mubalagha, and masdar sina‘i.
Pay attention to the examples 'raqbani' and 'sharani'.
2
Identify the two opinions about the 'ya'
The author presents two views: one holds that the 'ya' is for nisba (relative) with intensification via alif-nun; the other claims it is the 'ya' of the artificial infinitive (masdar sina‘i). Find and contrast these opinions in the text.
The second view is attributed to an unnamed scholar.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author refutes the second view by stating that a thing can be attributed to itself for intensification. Consider why this refutation works linguistically.
The principle is that nisba to the thing itself can emphasize its quality.
4
Apply the pattern to similar words
Think of other Arabic words with the pattern fa‘lani (like 'sharani' and 'raqbani'). How do they demonstrate mubalagha? This reinforces the author's explanation.
This step helps internalize the morphological rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أوَّلاً : أنَّ الوَحْدانية - بفتح الواو وسكون الحاء - نسبة إلى الوحدة، فهذه «الياء» التي فيها ياء النسبة ، وقد زيدت الألف والنون قبل «الياء» لإفادة المبالغة، كما زيدت في «رقباني» و «شعراني» وهما منسوبان إلى (الرقبة) و(الشعر). وقيل: لا يصح كون «الياء» للنسبة ، لأنَّ المراد ثبوت الوحدة نفسها ، لا ثبوت شيء منسوب إليها ، وصاحب هذا القيل استظهر أنَّ الياء التي في «الوحدانية» ياء المصدر الصناعي» مثل: الضاربية، والعالمية، والمفهومية ونحوها ، وهو مردودٌ بأنَّ الشيء قد ينسب لنفسه لقصد المبالغة. ثمَّ اعلم أن مبحث الوحدانية أشرفُ مباحث علم التوحيد ، ولذلك تجد العلماء قد اختاروا لهذا العلم اسما مشتقا من الوحدة فسموه: «علم التوحيد» وكثر في القرآن الكريم التنبيه على هذا المبحث والإشارة إليه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِلَهُكُمْ إِلَهُ وَحِدٌ لَّا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الرَّحْمَنُ الرَّحِيمُ ) [البقرة: ١٦٣] وقال جل شأنه: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد: ۱۹] ، وقال سبحانه: ﴿إِنَّنِي أَنَا اللهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا أَنَا [ طه : ١٤] وقال تعالت كلماته: ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا ءَالِهَةً إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا [الأنبياء: ٢٢] ، إلى غير ذلك من الآيات.
Know firstly: that al-wahdaniyya – with fat-ha on the waw and sukun on the ha – is a relative noun (nisba) to al-wahda (oneness). So this 'ya' in it is the ya of nisba, and the alif and nun have been added before the 'ya' to indicate intensification (mubalagha), just as they were added in 'raqbani' and 'sharani', both of which are relative to (al-raqaba) (the neck) and (al-sha'r) (hair). And it is said: it is not correct for the 'ya' to be for nisba, because what is intended is the affirmation of oneness itself, not the affirmation of something relative to it. The holder of this view asserted that the ya in 'al-wahdaniyya' is the ya of the artificial infinitive (al-masdar al-sina'i), like al-daribiyya, al-alamiyya, al-mafhumiyya and the like. But this is refuted by the fact that a thing may be attributed to itself for the purpose of intensification. Then know that the topic of al-wahdaniyya is the noblest of the topics of the science of tawhid, and for that reason you find the scholars have chosen for this science a name derived from al-wahda, so they named it 'Ilm al-Tawhid'. And in the Noble Quran the alerting to this topic and the indication to it are abundant. Allah Most High said: 'And your God is one God; there is no god but He, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' [al-Baqara: 163]. And He, majestic is His affair, said: 'So know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad: 19]. And He, exalted is He, said: 'Indeed, I am Allah; there is no god but Me' [Taha: 14]. And He, whose words are exalted, said: 'If there were in them other gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' [al-Anbiya': 22], and other such verses.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides a linguistic analysis of the term 'wahdaniyya' and then establishes its central importance in Islamic theology. The author begins with the morphological breakdown: the word is a relative adjective (nisba) formed from 'al-wahda' (oneness), with the characteristic 'ya' of nisba. The addition of 'alif' and 'nun' before this 'ya' serves for intensification (mubalagha), as in 'raqbani' (a person with a thick neck) and 'sharani' (a person with much hair). Then he presents an opposing view: some claim the 'ya' is that of the artificial infinitive (masdar sina'i), like 'daribiyya' (the quality of being a striker). The author rejects this, arguing that a noun can be attributed to itself for emphasis. Having clarified the word's meaning, he explains that the subject of wahdaniyya is the most honorable topic in the science of tawhid, so much so that the entire field is named after it (ilm al-tawhid). He supports this with four Quranic verses that command and assert the oneness of God, showing the scriptural foundation for this principle.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
ياء النسبة
المبالغة
المصدر الصناعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'wahdaniyya' is a masdar sina‘i (abstract verbal noun) rather than a relative adjective with intensification.
Correction: The author shows that the alif-nun pattern is used for mubalagha in nisba formations, and that a thing can be attributed to itself for emphasis, so the word remains a nisba with intensification.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the alif and nun added before the 'ya' in the word 'wahdaniyya'?
A) To indicate the dual form
B) To make it a masdar sina'i
C) To indicate intensification (mubalagha)
D) To change the word to a plural

Key Takeaway
Al-wahdaniyya is a relative adjective derived from 'al-wahda' with an intensifying alif-nun, emphasizing absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that 'wahdaniyya' is not a mere abstract concept but an intensified relative term—it seeps into every part of your existence. Is your belief in Allah's oneness an intense reality that shapes your every thought and action, or is it a comfortable, distant idea that leaves your daily choices untouched? What one concrete change can you make today to live with the intensity this word demands?
?
The author refutes the view that oneness itself is the direct meaning, insisting instead on the relational intensifier. When you say 'Allah is one,' do you feel the weight of that statement as a relational truth that binds you to Him utterly, or do you recite it as a lifeless formula? Will you pause before every prayer today to remind yourself that His oneness is not just a fact but a claim on your entire life?
?
If the word 'wahdaniyya' carries such strength that it attributes oneness to itself for intensification, how strong is your own attachment to that oneness? Are there 'partners' in your heart—love of status, wealth, or comfort—that dilute your devotion? What is the first step you will take tonight to sever one of those attachments and restore the intensity of your tawhid?

The Centrality of Tawhid in Islamic Theology
Establishes tawhid as the noblest topic and cites Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize why the science of theology is named after tawhid and see the Quranic foundation for the doctrine of God's oneness.

Core Idea
The science of tawhid is named after the very subject of God's oneness, the noblest topic.

Having clarified the term al-wahdaniyya, the author now explains its supreme importance in Islamic theology. The entire field is named 'ilm al-tawhid because this subject is the most honored. He then provides Quranic verses that command monotheism and demonstrate the impossibility of multiple gods, showing the scriptural basis for this cornerstone of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ثمَّ اعلم' to the end along with the translation. Notice the claim about the nobility of this topic and the naming of the science.
The author uses a logical connector 'ثمَّ' to transition.
2
Understand why the science is called 'Ilm al-Tawhid'
The author states that scholars chose a name derived from al-wahda (oneness) because the topic of wahdaniyya is the noblest. Reflect on why tawhid is the central theme.
This shows the preeminence of tawhid over other theological topics.
3
Examine the four Quranic verses
Read each verse: al-Baqara 163, Muhammad 19, Taha 14, and al-Anbiya' 22. For each, note what it contributes: declaration, command, divine speech, and logical argument.
The verse from al-Anbiya' provides a rational proof for tawhid.
4
Connect the textual evidence to the importance
The abundance of Quranic verses on tawhid reinforces its centrality. Consider how the Quran repeatedly emphasizes this doctrine.
The author ends with 'to other such verses' indicating many more.

اعلم أوَّلاً : أنَّ الوَحْدانية - بفتح الواو وسكون الحاء - نسبة إلى الوحدة، فهذه «الياء» التي فيها ياء النسبة ، وقد زيدت الألف والنون قبل «الياء» لإفادة المبالغة، كما زيدت في «رقباني» و «شعراني» وهما منسوبان إلى (الرقبة) و(الشعر). وقيل: لا يصح كون «الياء» للنسبة ، لأنَّ المراد ثبوت الوحدة نفسها ، لا ثبوت شيء منسوب إليها ، وصاحب هذا القيل استظهر أنَّ الياء التي في «الوحدانية» ياء المصدر الصناعي» مثل: الضاربية، والعالمية، والمفهومية ونحوها ، وهو مردودٌ بأنَّ الشيء قد ينسب لنفسه لقصد المبالغة. ثمَّ اعلم أن مبحث الوحدانية أشرفُ مباحث علم التوحيد ، ولذلك تجد العلماء قد اختاروا لهذا العلم اسما مشتقا من الوحدة فسموه: «علم التوحيد» وكثر في القرآن الكريم التنبيه على هذا المبحث والإشارة إليه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِلَهُكُمْ إِلَهُ وَحِدٌ لَّا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الرَّحْمَنُ الرَّحِيمُ ) [البقرة: ١٦٣] وقال جل شأنه: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد: ۱۹] ، وقال سبحانه: ﴿إِنَّنِي أَنَا اللهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا أَنَا [ طه : ١٤] وقال تعالت كلماته: ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا ءَالِهَةً إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا [الأنبياء: ٢٢] ، إلى غير ذلك من الآيات.
Know firstly: that al-wahdaniyya – with fat-ha on the waw and sukun on the ha – is a relative noun (nisba) to al-wahda (oneness). So this 'ya' in it is the ya of nisba, and the alif and nun have been added before the 'ya' to indicate intensification (mubalagha), just as they were added in 'raqbani' and 'sharani', both of which are relative to (al-raqaba) (the neck) and (al-sha'r) (hair). And it is said: it is not correct for the 'ya' to be for nisba, because what is intended is the affirmation of oneness itself, not the affirmation of something relative to it. And in the Noble Quran the alerting to this topic and the indication to it are abundant. And He, majestic is His affair, said: 'So know that there is no god but Allah' [Muhammad: 19]. And He, exalted is He, said: 'Indeed, I am Allah; there is no god but Me' [Taha: 14]. And He, whose words are exalted, said: 'If there were in them other gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' [al-Anbiya': 22], and other such verses.

The Primary Definition of Divine Will
Author defines irada and explains each part of the definition with refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard kalam definition of divine will, its five components, and how each component refutes specific theological errors.

Core Idea
Divine will is an eternal, real attribute that specifies possibilities.

The scholars of kalam define divine will with precision to protect against misunderstandings. In this passage, the author presents the standard definition and unpacks each term to show how it refutes heretical views. Pay attention to how each part of the definition serves as a refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition and its translation, noting the five components: attribute, pre-eternal, additional to essence, subsisting in essence, specifying the possible.
The definition is 'صفة أزليَّة زائدة على الذات ، قائمة به سبحانه ، تُخَصِّصُ الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه'
2
Identify the refutations for each term
For each component, the author explicitly names which group is refuted. Note who is refuted by 'pre-eternal', by 'additional to essence', etc.
The Karramiyya, Mu'tazila (various factions), and al-Najjar are mentioned.
3
Understand 'specifies the possible'
This phrase points to the eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim). Grasp that God's will pre-eternally specifies how each possible thing will be.
The author promises more detail in a later section.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Ask yourself why the definition is structured this way. Each term is carefully chosen to exclude a false view while affirming the correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام على هذه الصفة من الجهة الأولى فنقول: الإرادة في اللغة: القصد، وتُرادفها المشيئة ، وهي في اصطلاح علماء الكلام: صفة أزليَّة زائدة على الذات ، قائمة به سبحانه ، تُخَصِّصُ الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه ، فقولنا : (صفة) ، كالجنس في التعريف يشمل جميع الصفات، وقولنا: (قديمة) يراد بها الرَّدُّ على نحو الكرَّامية الذين قالوا: إنّ الإرادة صفة حادثة قائمة بذاته تعالى ، تقدَّسَ الله عما يقول المبطلون ، وقولنا : «زائدة على الذات»، يراد به الرَّدُّ على بعض المعتزلة الذين قالوا: إن الإرادة نفس الذات ، وقولنا: «قائمة بذاته تعالى» ، رد على فريقين من المعتزلة ، أولهما الجُبَّائي ومَن ذهب مذهبه من الذين قالوا: إنَّ الإرادة صفة قائمة بغير محلّ ، وثانيهما النَّجَّار ومَن ذهب مذهبه مِن الذين قالوا: إن الإرادة صفة سلبية ، وفسَّروها بأنَّها: عدم كون الفاعل ساهيًا أو مكرها ، وقد علمت فيما سبق أن الصفة السَّلبيَّة لا قيام لها ، لكونها أمرًا عدَميًّا ، وقولنا: تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه»، إشارة إلى التعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء أزلا بالصفات التي يعلم يوجد عليها في الخارج، وستعرفه مع سائر تعلقات الإرادة في الجهة الثالثة، وبعضهم عرف الإرادة بأنها: صفة تؤثر في اختصاص أحد طرفي الممكن، من وجود أو عدم، أو طول أو قصر ونحوها، بالوقوع بدلا عن مقابله»، فقوله: «صفة»، كالجنس في التعريف شامل لجميع الصَّفات، وقوله: «تؤثر»، كالفصل خرج به ما لا يؤثر مِن الصفات ، كالعلم والكلام والسمع والبصر والحياة ونحوها، ما عدا القدرة فإنها لا تخرج بهذا القيد، وقوله: في اختصاص» ، كفصل ثان خرج به القدرة ، والمراد بتخصيص أحد طرفي الممكن بالوقوع: ترجيح وقوع أحد طرفيه .
As for the discussion on this attribute from the first aspect, we say: al-irada in language is intention (al-qasd) and it is synonymous with al-mashi'a (volition). In the terminology of the scholars of kalam, it is a pre-eternal attribute, additional to the essence, subsisting in Him, may He be exalted, which specifies the possible (al-mumkin) with some of what is permissible for it. So our saying: (attribute) is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. And our saying: (pre-eternal) is intended to refute the Karramiyya who said that the will is an originated attribute subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, exalted is Allah above what the falsifiers say. And our saying: 'additional to the essence' is intended to refute some of the Mu'tazila who said that the will is the very essence. And our saying: 'subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted' is a refutation against two groups of the Mu'tazila: the first is al-Jubba'i and those who followed his doctrine, who said that the will is an attribute subsisting without a locus; and the second is al-Najjar and those who followed his doctrine, who said that the will is a negative attribute and interpreted it as: the absence of the agent being negligent or coerced. And you have known previously that the negative attribute has no subsistence, because it is a non-existent thing. And our saying: 'specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it' is an indication to the eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim), which is Allah's specification of a thing pre-eternally with the attributes upon which He knows it will exist externally, and you will know it along with the rest of the attachments of will in the third aspect. And some defined al-irada as: an attribute that effects the specification of one of the two sides of the possible (i.e., existence or non-existence, or length or shortness, and the like) to occur instead of its counterpart. So his saying: 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition encompassing all attributes. And his saying: 'effects' is like a differentia, by which is excluded that which does not effect from the attributes, such as knowledge, speech, hearing, sight, life, and the like, except for power (al-qudra), for it is not excluded by this restriction. And his saying: 'in the specification' is a second differentia, by which power is excluded. And the meaning of 'specification of one of the two sides of the possible to occur' is the preponderance of the occurrence of one of its two sides.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (al-irada). The author begins with the linguistic meaning (qasd/mashi'a) and then gives the technical kalam definition: a pre-eternal attribute, additional to the essence, subsisting in God, which specifies possible things. He then explains each term to show how it refutes heretical groups: 'pre-eternal' refutes the Karramiyya who said will is originated; 'additional to the essence' refutes Mu'tazila who said will is identical to the essence; 'subsisting in the essence' refutes al-Jubba'i (will without a locus) and al-Najjar (will as a negative attribute, which is non-existent). The phrase 'specifies the possible with some of what is permissible' points to the eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim). Then an alternative definition is given: will is an attribute that effects the specification of one of two sides of a possible. In that definition, 'attribute' is the genus, 'effects' excludes non-effecting attributes like knowledge, and 'in specification' excludes power (since power effects but not specifically specification). The meaning of specification is preponderance of one side.

إرادة
صفة أزلية
كرامية
معتزلة
جبّائي
نجّار
صفة سلبية
تعلق تنجيزي قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the divine will is temporary or originates in time, like human will.
Correction: The definition explicitly says 'pre-eternal' (azaliyya) and refutes the Karramiyya who held this view.

Checkpoint
Which group does the term 'pre-eternal' in the definition of irada specifically refute?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) The Karramiyya
C) Al-Najjar and his followers

Key Takeaway
The definition of divine will is a precise theological tool that simultaneously affirms God's real attribute and refutes multiple heresies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will controlling every detail of your life, do you sometimes feel it is distant or impersonal? This definition says it is a real, eternal, and specific determination. How does realizing that God's will is not a vague force but a precise and personal decree change the way you face your daily decisions? What is one area where you will consciously submit to His specific will today?
?
The author refutes the idea that God's will is merely the absence of neglect (as al-Najjar claimed). Are there areas in your life where you treat God's involvement as passive, thinking He simply doesn't interfere? How does knowing that His will actively specifies everything jolt you out of that comfort? What specific thought or action will you adjust starting now?
?
If the divine will is a real attribute distinct from God's essence (as refuting those who identify them), then God is not simply a force but a personal being with real will. When you pray, do you address a Person with a genuine will that listens and responds, or a distant concept? What needs to change in your prayer to reflect this truth, beginning with your next salah?

The Alternative Definition: Will as an Effecting Attribute
Author presents an alternative definition of irada and explains its logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative definition of divine will as an attribute that effects specification, and how it distinguishes will from other attributes like power.

Core Idea
Will is an attribute that makes one possibility occur over another.

In addition to the primary definition, some scholars define divine will more succinctly as an attribute that brings about the specification of one of two possible outcomes. This definition clarifies the unique role of will compared to other attributes, especially power. Read carefully to see how the terms 'attributes', 'effects', and 'in specification' act as genus and differentiae.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative definition
Read the Arabic and translation: 'an attribute that effects the specification of one of the two sides of the possible'.
The definition is: 'صفة تؤثر في اختصاص أحد طرفي الممكن بالوقوع بدلا عن مقابله'
2
Analyze the logical structure
Notice that 'attribute' is the genus, 'effects' is a differentia excluding non-effecting attributes, and 'in specification' is a second differentia excluding power.
Power effects but not specifically specification.
3
Understand 'specification'
Specification means preponderance (tarjih) of one side (existence, non-existence, length, etc.) over its counterpart.
4
Compare with the primary definition
Reflect on how this definition complements the earlier one by focusing on the effect of will rather than its eternal nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام على هذه الصفة من الجهة الأولى فنقول: الإرادة في اللغة: القصد، وتُرادفها المشيئة ، وهي في اصطلاح علماء الكلام: صفة أزليَّة زائدة على الذات ، قائمة به سبحانه ، تُخَصِّصُ الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه ، فقولنا : (صفة) ، كالجنس في التعريف يشمل جميع الصفات، وقولنا: (قديمة) يراد بها الرَّدُّ على نحو الكرَّامية الذين قالوا: إنّ الإرادة صفة حادثة قائمة بذاته تعالى ، تقدَّسَ الله عما يقول المبطلون ، وقولنا : «زائدة على الذات»، يراد به الرَّدُّ على بعض المعتزلة الذين قالوا: إن الإرادة نفس الذات ، وقولنا: «قائمة بذاته تعالى» ، رد على فريقين من المعتزلة ، أولهما الجُبَّائي ومَن ذهب مذهبه من الذين قالوا: إنَّ الإرادة صفة قائمة بغير محلّ ، وثانيهما النَّجَّار ومَن ذهب مذهبه مِن الذين قالوا: إن الإرادة صفة سلبية ، وفسَّروها بأنَّها: عدم كون الفاعل ساهيًا أو مكرها ، وقد علمت فيما سبق أن الصفة السَّلبيَّة لا قيام لها ، لكونها أمرًا عدَميًّا ، وقولنا: تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه»، إشارة إلى التعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء أزلا بالصفات التي يعلم يوجد عليها في الخارج، وستعرفه مع سائر تعلقات الإرادة في الجهة الثالثة، وبعضهم عرف الإرادة بأنها: صفة تؤثر في اختصاص أحد طرفي الممكن، من وجود أو عدم، أو طول أو قصر ونحوها، بالوقوع بدلا عن مقابله»، فقوله: «صفة»، كالجنس في التعريف شامل لجميع الصَّفات، وقوله: «تؤثر»، كالفصل خرج به ما لا يؤثر مِن الصفات ، كالعلم والكلام والسمع والبصر والحياة ونحوها، ما عدا القدرة فإنها لا تخرج بهذا القيد، وقوله: في اختصاص» ، كفصل ثان خرج به القدرة ، والمراد بتخصيص أحد طرفي الممكن بالوقوع: ترجيح وقوع أحد طرفيه .
As for the discussion on this attribute from the first aspect, we say: al-irada in language is intention (al-qasd) and it is synonymous with al-mashi'a (volition). In the terminology of the scholars of kalam, it is a pre-eternal attribute, additional to the essence, subsisting in Him, may He be exalted, which specifies the possible (al-mumkin) with some of what is permissible for it. So our saying: (attribute) is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. And our saying: (pre-eternal) is intended to refute the Karramiyya who said that the will is an originated attribute subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, exalted is Allah above what the falsifiers say. And our saying: 'additional to the essence' is intended to refute some of the Mu'tazila who said that the will is the very essence. And our saying: 'subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted' is a refutation against two groups of the Mu'tazila: the first is al-Jubba'i and those who followed his doctrine, who said that the will is an attribute subsisting without a locus; and the second is al-Najjar and those who followed his doctrine, who said that the will is a negative attribute and interpreted it as: the absence of the agent being negligent or coerced. And you have known previously that the negative attribute has no subsistence, because it is a non-existent thing. And our saying: 'specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it' is an indication to the eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim), which is Allah's specification of a thing pre-eternally with the attributes upon which He knows it will exist externally, and you will know it along with the rest of the attachments of will in the third aspect. And some defined al-irada as: an attribute that effects the specification of one of the two sides of the possible (i.e., existence or non-existence, or length or shortness, and the like) to occur instead of its counterpart. So his saying: 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition encompassing all attributes. And his saying: 'effects' is like a differentia, by which is excluded that which does not effect from the attributes, such as knowledge, speech, hearing, sight, life, and the like, except for power (al-qudra), for it is not excluded by this restriction. And his saying: 'in the specification' is a second differentia, by which power is excluded. And the meaning of 'specification of one of the two sides of the possible to occur' is the preponderance of the occurrence of one of its two sides.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (al-irada). The author begins with the linguistic meaning (qasd/mashi'a) and then gives the technical kalam definition: a pre-eternal attribute, additional to the essence, subsisting in God, which specifies possible things. He then explains each term to show how it refutes heretical groups: 'pre-eternal' refutes the Karramiyya who said will is originated; 'additional to the essence' refutes Mu'tazila who said will is identical to the essence; 'subsisting in the essence' refutes al-Jubba'i (will without a locus) and al-Najjar (will as a negative attribute, which is non-existent). The phrase 'specifies the possible with some of what is permissible' points to the eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim). Then an alternative definition is given: will is an attribute that effects the specification of one of two sides of a possible. In that definition, 'attribute' is the genus, 'effects' excludes non-effecting attributes like knowledge, and 'in specification' excludes power (since power effects but not specifically specification). The meaning of specification is preponderance of one side.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
صفة
اختصاص
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine will and divine power are the same because both 'effect' things.
Correction: Power effects creation generally, but will specifically determines which possibility becomes actual. The second differentia 'in specification' excludes power.

Checkpoint
According to the alternative definition, which attribute is excluded only by the second differentia ('in specification') and not by the first differentia?
A) Knowledge
B) Life
C) Power
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
The definition of will as an effecting attribute distinguishes its role from other attributes like power by focusing on the specification of possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision between two seemingly equal options, do you consciously recognize that Allah's will (irada) is what ultimately specifies the outcome? How will you remind yourself of this before your next choice?
?
The definition distinguishes will from power: will specifies, power executes. In your life, do you confuse desiring something (will) with being able to achieve it (power)? How can you realign your planning to trust Allah's specification rather than your own capability?
?
If Allah's will effects specification, then every detail of your day—from waking up to the events you encounter—is specifically chosen. Will you commit today to see each event as a deliberate act of divine specification and respond with gratitude or patience?

Defining Speech and Proving Internal Speech
Defines speech, contrasts Mu'tazila and Sunni views, and proves internal speech is real and called kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of speech as including internal speech, and the arguments proving that internal speech is a real attribute distinct from mere ability.

Core Idea
Speech is not limited to sounds; it begins in the heart.

This lesson explores the foundational debate about the nature of speech. The Mu'tazila insisted that speech is only audible sounds and letters, but the Ahl al-Sunna maintain that speech includes an internal reality—inner speech (حديث النفس). We will examine the evidence from human experience, Arabic language, and logical reasoning that establishes internal speech as a real attribute, and see how this shapes our understanding of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Disagreement
Read the opening lines to see how the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna differ on the definition of speech.
The Mu'tazila restrict speech to sound+letter; the Sunnis include inner speech.
2
Examine the Evidence for Inner Speech
Identify the three proofs: human self-awareness, the poet Al-Akhtal's line, and the argument that inner speech is additional to power.
The first two prove existence and naming; the third proves it is a distinct attribute.
3
Apply to God
Reflect on how the argument for human inner speech opens the door to understanding God's speech as not requiring sound or letters.
God describes Himself as 'speaking' (متكلم), which must mean He possesses internal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخطوة الأولى في هذه المسألة تحديد معنى الكلام، وهل يتعيَّنُ أَلَّا يكون كلاما إلا ما كان بصوت وحرف أو لا ذلك ؟ يتعين أما المعتزلة فذهبوا إلى أنه لا يسمى كلاماً إلا ما كان بصوت وحرف. وأما أهل السُّنَّة فقالوا: يطلق لفظ الكلام على شيئين: الأول: حديثُ النَّفس، والثاني: الأصوات والحروف المُترتِّبة ، ولا سبيل إلى إنكار المعنى الأول في حقٌّ الإنسان، ولا في أنه يُسمى كلاماً ، ولا في أنَّه زائد على القدرة والإرادة والعلم، ألا ترى أن الإنسان يقول: زورت البارحة في نفسي كلاماً ، وأنا نقول في نفس فلان كلام وهو يريد أن ينطق به ، فأما أن ذلك ثابت في حق الإنسان، فإن كل واحد منا يشعر من نفسه به ، وأما أنه يسمى كلاما فقد سماه العرب الذين بلغتهم نزل القرآن كلاماً ومن ذلك قول الأَخْطَل التَّغْلِبي: لَا يُعْجِبَنَّكَ مِن خَطِيبٍ خُطْبَةٌ حتَّى يَكُونَ مَعَ الْكَلَامِ أَصِيلًا إِنَّ الْكَلَامَ لَفِي الْفُؤَادِ ، وَإِنَّمَا جُعِلَ اللَّسَانُ عَلَى الفُؤَادِ دَلِيلًا وأمَّا أَنَّه زائد على القدرة فلأن معنى القدرة على الكلام لا يزيد على الصلاحية، وقد علمنا أنه لا يقال على من صَلُحَ لشيء إنه متصف بذلك الشيء ؛ فمَن صَلُحَ للعلم لا يقال له: عالمٌ ، ومَن صلح للسمع لا يقال له: سامع ، ومن صلح للحمل ، لا يقال له: حامل ، وهكذا، وإذا كان كلام الإنسان ليس مقصوراً على ما كان بصوت وحرف فليكن الله تعالى كلام، وليكن سبحانه موصوفا بأنه متكلم ، من غير أن يستلزم ذلك أن يكون كلامه بصوت وحرف، وقد وجدْنَا الله تعالى وصف نفسه بأنَّه متكلّم، فعلمنا من ذلك أنَّه لا يعني بذلك أنه قادر على الكلام.
The first step in this issue is defining the meaning of speech (الكلام), and whether it is necessarily not speech except what is with sound and letter, or not that? It is determined. As for the Mu'tazila, they held that nothing is called speech except what is with sound and letter. And as for the Ahl al-Sunna, they said: the word speech (الكلام) is applied to two things: first: inner speech (حديث النفس), and second: the arranged sounds and letters. There is no way to deny the first meaning in the case of man, nor that it is called speech, nor that it is additional (زائد) to power (القدرة), will (الإرادة), and knowledge (العلم). Do you not see that a man says: 'I composed (زورت) last night in my soul speech,' and we say: 'In the soul of so-and-so is speech while he intends to utter it.' As for that being established in the case of man, every one of us feels it from himself. As for it being called speech, the Arabs in whose language the Qur'an was revealed called it speech, and among that is the saying of Al-Akhtal al-Taghlibi: 'Let not the speech (خطبة) of an orator impress you until he is with his speech (الكلام) well-grounded. Indeed, speech (الكلام) is in the heart (الفؤاد), and the tongue was only made a guide to the heart.' And as for it being additional to power (القدرة), because the meaning of power over speech does not exceed fitness (الصلاحية), and we know that one who is fit for a thing is not said to be described with that thing. So whoever is fit for knowledge is not called 'knowing' (عالم), and whoever is fit for hearing is not called 'hearing' (سامع), and whoever is fit for carrying is not called 'carrier' (حامل), and so on. And if man's speech is not limited to what is with sound and letter, then let God's speech be [كلام], and let Him, exalted, be described as speaking (متكلم), without that necessitating that His speech be with sound and letter. And we have found God, exalted, describing Himself as speaking (متكلم), so we know from that that He does not mean by that that He is capable (قادر) of speech.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by setting up the first step: defining speech. It poses the question: is speech necessarily only that which consists of sound and letter? The Mu'tazila answer 'yes'. The Ahl al-Sunna answer 'no' and present a two-part definition: (1) inner speech (حديث النفس) and (2) uttered sounds and letters. They argue that inner speech cannot be denied in humans because everyone experiences it (e.g., composing speech in the mind, or saying someone has speech in his heart). They prove it is called speech by citing the Arab poet Al-Akhtal, who explicitly says 'speech is in the heart.' They also prove that this inner speech is an additional attribute (زائد) beyond mere power (قدرة) because power only indicates fitness (صلاحية), not actual actuality; for example, being fit for knowledge does not make one actually knowledgeable. Since human speech includes this non-sound, non-letter component, then God's speech can be similarly non-sound and non-letter. Moreover, God describes Himself as 'speaking' (متكلم), so He is not merely 'capable of speech'.

Key Terms
الكلام
حديث النفس
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الصلاحية
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Internal speech is the same as the ability to speak (power).
Correction: Internal speech is an actual attribute distinct from power; power only indicates fitness, not actual speech.

Checkpoint
What did the Ahl al-Sunna use as evidence that internal speech is called kalam in Arabic?
A) A verse from the Qur'an
B) A hadith of the Prophet
C) A line of poetry by Al-Akhtal
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Internal speech is a real attribute of humans and God, distinct from uttered words and from mere power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever planned a conversation in your heart before speaking, only to realize that your inner words were full of pride, anger, or falsehood? How does knowing that inner speech is real and accountable change the way you guard your thoughts?
?
If God's speech is not sounds or letters, but an eternal, living reality in His essence, what does that mean for how you approach the Quran? Are you content to merely recite its letters, or are you seeking the inner speech of God that those letters point to?
?
The Mu'tazila denied inner speech because they could not imagine speech without sound. What attributes of God have you unknowingly limited by your own human imagination? Will you let God be God, or do you insist on defining Him by your own categories?

Kalam as an Actual Attribute Beyond Qu dra
Author defines kalam linguistically and argues it is additional to qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that kalam (divine speech) is an actual attribute distinct from the mere power to speak.

Core Idea
Speech is an actual attribute, not just the capacity to speak.

This passage begins by establishing the linguistic meaning of kalam, citing an Arab poet to show that speech originates in the heart. The author then argues that kalam is additional to qudra, using analogies to illustrate that suitability does not equal possession. This distinction is crucial for understanding Allah's attribute of speech without anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic source and its English translation. Notice the poetic citation in the first part and the logical argument in the second.
The poetry shows that Arabs understood speech as something internal.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the sentence where the author says kalam is 'zā'id' (additional) to qudra. Understand the analogy: suitability for something does not mean one possesses it.
Examples: suitability for knowledge ≠ being knowing.
3
Reflect on the implication for Allah's speech
Consider the conclusion: Allah’s speech is not composed of sound or letters, yet He is described as mutakallim (speaking) as a real attribute.
This avoids anthropomorphism while affirming the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد سماه العرب الذين بلغتهم نزل القرآن كلاماً ومن ذلك قول الأَخْطَل التَّغْلِبي: لَا يُعْجِبَنَّكَ مِن خَطِيبٍ خُطْبَةٌ حتَّى يَكُونَ مَعَ الْكَلَامِ أَصِيلًا إِنَّ الْكَلَامَ لَفِي الْفُؤَادِ ، وَإِنَّمَا جُعِلَ اللَّسَانُ عَلَى الفُؤَادِ دَلِيلًا وأمَّا أَنَّه زائد على القدرة فلأن معنى القدرة على الكلام لا يزيد على الصلاحية، وقد علمنا أنه لا يقال على من صَلُحَ لشيء إنه متصف بذلك الشيء ؛ فمَن صَلُحَ للعلم لا يقال له: عالمٌ ، ومَن صلح للسمع لا يقال له: سامع ، ومن صلح للحمل ، لا يقال له: حامل ، وهكذا، وإذا كان كلام الإنسان ليس مقصوراً عل ما كان بصوت وحرف فليكن الله تعالى كلام، وليكن سبحانه موصوفا بأنه متكلم ، من غير أن يستلزم ذلك أن يكون كلامه بصوت وحرف، وقد وجدْنَا الله تعالى وصف نفسه بأنَّه متكلّم، فعلمنا من ذلك أنَّه لا يعني بذلك أنه قادر على الكلام.
For the Arabs to whom the Qur'an was revealed called it speech (kalam). Among that is the saying of Al-Akhtal al-Taghlibi: 'Let not a speaker's speech impress you until he is sound with speech. Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only made a guide to the heart.' And as for it being additional to power (qudra), because the meaning of power over speech does not exceed suitability, and we know that one who is suitable for something is not said to be qualified by that thing; so one who is suitable for knowledge is not called 'knowing', and one suitable for hearing is not called 'hearing', and one suitable for carrying is not called 'carrying', and so on. And if human speech is not confined to what is with sound and letter, then let the speech of Allah be speech, and let Him be described as speaking (mutakallim), without that necessitating that His speech be with sound and letter. And we have found Allah describing Himself as 'speaking', so we know from that that He does not mean thereby that He is powerful over speech.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'kalam' (speech) and establishes it as an additional attribute beyond mere power (qudra). The author begins by citing a line of Arabic poetry from Al-Akhtal, showing that the Arabs understood speech as something in the heart, not just uttered sounds. This lexicographical point supports the idea that speech can be non-sonic. The author then argues that kalam is additional to qudra because qudra only indicates suitability, not actual possession. For example, someone suitable for knowledge is not called 'knowing' until knowledge actually exists. Similarly, Allah describes Himself as 'speaking', which implies an attribute beyond mere power. Since human speech is not limited to sound and letter, Allah's speech is even more exalted and without sound or letter. The passage concludes that Allah's self-description as 'speaking' does not mean He is merely capable of speech, but that He actually possesses the attribute of speech.

Key Terms
كلام
قدرة
صلاحية
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that kalam is merely the capacity to speak (i.e., identical to qudra).
Correction: The passage shows that kalam is an actual attribute beyond mere capacity, because suitability does not equal possession, and Allah describes Himself as actually speaking.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the relationship between kalam (speech) and qudra (power)?
A) Kalam is identical to qudra.
B) Kalam is additional to qudra and is an actual attribute.
C) Kalam is only qudra under certain conditions.
D) Kalam is the same as the ability to produce sounds.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is a real divine attribute, not just the power to speak, as proven by language, reason, and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Qur'an, do you truly feel that Allah is speaking to you personally, or do you treat it merely as a book of guidance? What would change in your heart if you believed every verse is His direct speech?
?
The poet said, 'Indeed speech is in the heart.' Have you ever considered that your own inner thoughts and intentions are 'speech' to Allah? Are you honest with Him in what you think and feel, knowing He hears the heart's speech?
?
If Allah's attribute of speech is real and additional to His power, then His words are not just possibilities but actual communications. How does this reality press you to respond to the Qur'an with more than passive listening? What specific verse will you obey today because it is His actual command?

The Two Meanings of Divine Speech
Defines the two types of divine speech and their linguistic classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between Allah's internal (nafsi) and verbal (lafzi) speech, and how they are classified as homonymy, equivocality, or literal/figurative usage.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is both an eternal attribute and a created expression—each is distinct.

Ahl al-Sunnah distinguishes two aspects of divine speech to avoid confusing Allah's eternal attribute with His temporal revelation. This lesson unpacks their definitions and the linguistic terms that describe their relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and locate the definitions of internal speech (kalam nafsi qadim) and verbal speech (kalam lafzi). Notice that one is eternal and subsisting in Allah, the other is created and not subsisting.
The verbal speech is created by Allah upon a specific order, with no human acquisition in its composition.
2
Understand the linguistic classification
The passage offers three possible classifications for the relationship between the two meanings: homonymy (mushtarak lafzi), equivocality (mushtarak ma'nawi), or literal/figurative (haqiqa/majaz). Reflect on the implications of each.
The author inclines toward the literal/figurative view: 'it is literal in the internal and figurative in the verbal'.
3
Ask why this distinction matters
Consider how failing to distinguish these two meanings could lead to theological errors, such as attributing creation to an eternal attribute or denying the divine origin of the recited Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أهل السنة والجماعة بناءً على أصلهم الأوَّلِ كلامُ الله تعالى يطلق على: الكلام النفسي القديم الذي هو صفة قديمة قائمة بذاته تعالى، ويطلق أيضا على الكلام اللفظي لا بمعنى أنه قائم به، بل بمعنى أنه خلقه على نظمه، وليس لأحد في أصل تركيبه كسب، وإطلاقه على هذين المعنيين من قبيل المشترك اللفظي، أو من قبيل المشترك المعنوي، أو من قبيل الحقيقة والمجاز؛ فهو حقيقة في النفسي مجاز في اللفظي من باب إطلاق اللفظ على الدال. والقرآن الكريم الذي نتلوه بألسنتنا ونكتبه ويسمعه بعضنا من بعض هو: كلام الله تعالى اللفظي، على المعنى الذي ذكرناه، وهو المعنى الذي يسمعه المستجير بالرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكل من أنكر أن ما بين دفتي المصحف كلام الله فهو كافر، نعم، لو قال قائل: إن ما بين دفتي المصحف ليس كلام الله، وهو يريد أن ما بين دفتي المصحف ليس هو الصفة القائمة بذاته تعالى لم يكفر بذلك، وقد نهى العلماء أن يقال: «القرآن حادث» اعترافهم بكون الألفاظ المتلوة والكتابة حادثة، والسِّرُّ في هذا النهي أنَّ لفظ القرآن يطلق مجازا على الصفة القائمة بذات الله تعالى، فخافوا أن يتوهم متوهم أنَّ المقصود بقول القائل: (القرآن حادث) أنَّ الصفة القائمة بذاته حادثة، فلدفع هذا التوهم نَهوا عن أن تطلق هذه العبارة إلا في مقام التعليم مع بيان ما يراد بها، وهذا يفسر لنا إصرار شيخ السنة الإمام أحمد بن حنبل على أن يقول: القرآن قديم ورضاه بالضرب والتعذيب، والسَّجن، في سبيل هذا الإصرار وامتناعه أنْ يقولَ: «القرآن حادث؛ لأنه خافَ أن يفهم العامةُ هذا المعنى الذي تُوهِمُه هذه العبارة؛ ويكون هو قدوتهم في هذا وحجتهم عند الله تعالى، رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه، وأثابه عن وتورعه أحسنَ ما يُجزي به المُخلصين.
And Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said, based upon their first principle: Allah's speech is applied to: the pre-eternal internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim) which is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and it is also applied to verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi), not in the sense that it subsists in Him, but in the sense that He created it upon its order, and no one has any acquisition in its original composition. And its application to these two meanings is from the category of homonymy (al-mushtarak al-lafzi), or from the category of equivocality (al-mushtarak al-ma'nawi), or from the category of literal and figurative usage (al-haqiqa wa al-majaz); so it is literal in the internal and figurative in the verbal, from the category of applying the word to the signifier. And the noble Qur'an which we recite with our tongues and write and hear from one another is: the verbal speech of Allah, according to the meaning we mentioned, and it is the meaning that the one seeking protection with the Messenger (peace be upon him) hears. And whoever denies that what is between the covers of the mushaf is the speech of Allah is a disbeliever. Yes, if someone says: 'What is between the covers of the mushaf is not the speech of Allah,' intending that what is between the covers of the mushaf is not the attribute subsisting in His essence, he does not become a disbeliever. And the scholars forbade that one say: 'The Qur'an is created,' acknowledging that the recited words and writing are created. The secret behind this prohibition is that the term 'Qur'an' is applied figuratively to the attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, so they feared that someone might mistakenly think that the intended meaning of the one saying 'The Qur'an is created' is that the attribute subsisting in His essence is created. So to repel this misunderstanding, they forbade uttering this expression except in a teaching context with clarification of what is intended by it. And this explains to us the insistence of the master of the Sunnah, Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, to say: 'The Qur'an is eternal,' and his acceptance of beating, torture, and imprisonment for the sake of this insistence, and his refusal to say: 'The Qur'an is created,' because he feared that the common people would understand this meaning that this expression suggests, and he would be their model in this and their argument before Allah. May Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him, and reward him for his piety the best reward with which He repays the sincere.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the two meanings of divine speech as held by Ahl al-Sunnah. It begins by stating that Allah's speech (kalam Allah) is used for two things: (1) the pre-eternal internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi al-qadim), which is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence; and (2) verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi), which is not subsisting in Him but is rather created by Him with a specific order, and no one has any acquisition (kasb) in its original composition. The relation between these two meanings is then classified: it is either homonymy (mushtarak lafzi), equivocality (mushtarak ma'nawi), or literal vs. figurative (haqiqa/majaz) with the internal being literal and the verbal figurative (by applying the name of the signifier to the sign). The passage then turns to the noble Qur'an that we recite, write, and hear: it is Allah's verbal speech in the sense mentioned. Whoever denies that what is between the covers of the mushaf is Allah's speech becomes a disbeliever (kafir). However, if someone says 'What is between the covers is not Allah's speech' meaning specifically that it is not the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, then he does not become a disbeliever. This clarifies that the denial of the Qur'an as speech is only kufr if one rejects it as Allah's speech altogether, not if one distinguishes the two modes. The passage then discusses the prohibition against saying 'The Qur'an is created' (al-Qur'an hadith). The scholars forbade this phrase even though the recited words and writing are indeed created, because the term 'Qur'an' is also used figuratively for the eternal attribute. Thus, saying 'the Qur'an is created' could be misunderstood to mean that the eternal attribute is created. To prevent this confusion, the utterance is prohibited except in an educational context with clarification. This explains Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's insistence on saying 'The Qur'an is eternal' and his endurance of punishment, because he feared that the common people would follow him in saying 'the Qur'an is created' and thus fall into error. The passage ends with a prayer for Imam Ahmad.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي
المشترك اللفظي
المشترك المعنوي
الحقيقة والمجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the term 'speech of Allah' (kalam Allah) is applied to how many distinct meanings?
A) One meaning only
B) Two meanings
C) Three meanings
D) Four meanings

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech has two dimensions: an eternal attribute (internal) and a created expression (verbal); the term is literal for the former and figurative for the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you consciously recognize that the words you utter are created expressions pointing to an eternal attribute of Allah? How does that awareness change the reverence and humility with which you read?
?
The passage teaches that the verbal speech is created by Allah and no one has any acquisition in its original composition. Does your approach to studying the Qur'an reflect a conviction that its ultimate Author is beyond your full comprehension, or do you subtly assume you can master it through your own intellect?
?
If the term 'speech of Allah' is literal for the internal attribute and figurative for the verbal expression, how does that distinction affect your understanding of the Qur'an's majesty? Are you in danger of treating the created words as if they were the eternal attribute itself, or of forgetting that they signify a greater reality?

The Qur'an: Created or Eternal?
Discusses the nature of the recited Qur'an, the ruling on denial, and the prohibition of saying 'the Qur'an is created'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the recited Qur'an is Allah's verbal speech, the ruling on denying it, the prohibition of saying 'the Qur'an is created', and Imam Ahmad's stance in the inquisition.

Core Idea
Calling the Qur'an 'created' risks misunderstanding; Imam Ahmad endured trials to protect the common belief.

The Qur'an we recite is created words, yet to call it 'created' without qualification can lead to heresy. This lesson explores the scholars' careful language and Imam Ahmad's steadfastness.

Guided Path
1
Define the recited Qur'an
Read the passage defining the noble Qur'an as Allah's verbal speech—recited, written, and heard. Note that this is the same speech heard by the one seeking protection with the Prophet.
2
Understand the ruling on denial
The passage states that denying that the mushaf (the physical copy) is Allah's speech constitutes disbelief—unless the denial intends that it is not the eternal attribute, in which case it does not.
This nuance is crucial: kufr is only if one rejects the Qur'an as Allah's speech altogether.
3
Learn the prohibition of 'the Qur'an is created'
Scholars forbade this phrase even though the words and writing are created, because the term 'Qur'an' also refers to the eternal attribute figuratively, leading to potential confusion.
The prohibition is to prevent misunderstanding, not because the statement is false per se.
4
Reflect on Imam Ahmad's stand
Imam Ahmad insisted on saying 'the Qur'an is eternal' and refused to say 'created', enduring punishment because he feared the common people would follow him and fall into error.
His piety and caution protected the correct belief about Allah's attribute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The physical Qur'an (mushaf) is eternal and uncreated.
Correction: The recited words and writing are created, but the attribute of speech (internal speech) is eternal. The term 'Qur'an' can refer to both, so caution is needed to avoid confusing the two.

A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He does not become a disbeliever.
C) He is considered a sinner but not a disbeliever.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
The recited Qur'an is Allah's verbal speech; denying it is kufr, but calling it 'created' requires caution to avoid attributing creation to the eternal attribute.

?

?
If your reputation or safety depended on saying something that might give a wrong impression about Allah’s nature, would you stand firm like Imam Ahmad? What is one practical step you can take today to guard your tongue when speaking about the Qur'an?

Definition of the Attribute of Hearing
Presents a scholarly definition of hearing and notes it will be explained later.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly definition of the attribute of hearing as a disclosing quality, similar to knowledge.

Core Idea
Hearing discloses things like knowledge does.

In this brief passage, the author presents a definition of the divine attribute of hearing offered by some scholars. This definition likens hearing to knowledge in its function of making things disclosed and clear. The author also notes that a fuller explanation will come later in the discussion on sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the original wording, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage is a single sentence containing a definition and a forward reference.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms 'صفة السمع', 'ينكشف', and 'العلم'. Understand their meanings in context.
'ينكشف' means 'is disclosed' or 'unveiled'.
3
Understand the comparison
The definition says hearing is like knowledge in that both disclose and clarify the thing they relate to. Grasp that this frames hearing beyond mere sound perception.
This comparison shows hearing as an illuminative attribute.
4
Note the anticipation
The author promises further explanation in the section on sight. Observe how this builds continuity in the text.
The connection between hearing and sight will be explored later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العلماء من عرَّف صفة السَّمع بقوله: صفة ينكشف بها الشيء ويتضح كالعلم وسنشرح هذا التعريف في الكلام على صفة البصر.
And among the scholars is one who defined the attribute of hearing by saying: 'An attribute by which the thing is disclosed and becomes clear, like knowledge.' And we will explain this definition in the discussion on the attribute of sight.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage gives a straightforward definition of the attribute of hearing (sam'). According to this scholar, hearing is a quality that makes a thing disclosed (inkishaf) and clear (tawaddun), akin to how knowledge discloses its object. The author notes that this definition will be further explained later, specifically in the section on the attribute of sight (al-basar).

Key Terms

ينكشف
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing is merely the perception of sounds.
Correction: The definition given here portrays hearing as an attribute that discloses and clarifies any thing, similar to knowledge, not limited to sounds.

Checkpoint

A) Both are acquired through learning
B) Both disclose the thing and make it clear
C) Both are limited to sounds
D) Both depend on physical organs

Key Takeaway
The attribute of hearing is defined as a quality that discloses and clarifies, analogous to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you allow that hearing to disclose its truth to your heart, or do you merely let the sounds pass by? What would change if you listened with the intention of active disclosure?
?
The scholars compare hearing to knowledge in that both disclose reality. In what area of your life have you been satisfied with surface-level hearing without seeking deeper understanding? Will you commit today to listen as a means of true knowledge?
?
Hearing is not passive but an attribute that clarifies. When someone gives you sincere advice, do you receive it as a disclosure that reveals something about yourself, or do you resist? How can you cultivate a hearing that leads to personal transformation?

What Is Kalam?

The Permissible in Divine Matters and Refutation of Deviant Views
Definition of al-ja'iz and refutation of two deviant groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ja'iz (the intellectually permissible) in relation to Allah and how it refutes the Mu'tazila and the Barahima.

Core Idea
What is possible for Allah includes all possibilities, refuting those who impose obligations or impossibilities.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes a foundational concept in Islamic theology: the nature of divine possibility. The author defines al-ja'iz (the permissible) in God's right, provides a concrete example, and then shows how this definition directly refutes two heretical groups. Understanding this will help you grasp the balance between divine freedom and the necessary limits of rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: definition, example, objection, and refutation of two groups.
The passage is dense but well-structured.
2
Understand the Definition
Focus on how al-ja'iz is defined as 'what is possible for us to do or leave' and how the author clarifies that this applies to Allah as bringing into existence or annihilating every possible thing.
Note the key terms: إيجاد (bringing into existence) and إعدام (annihilating).
3
Examine the Objection and Answer
The author addresses a potential tautology: if al-ja'iz equals al-mumkin, then the definition is circular. The answer is that the relative noun 'ma' indicates generality, so the predicate adds substantive meaning.
4
Identify the Two Refuted Groups
Identify the Mu'tazila (who claim some possibilities are obligatory) and the Barahima (who claim some possibilities are impossible). Understand how each is refuted by the definition.
This is the practical application of the definition.

قسم الإلهيات - وهو ما يصح في نظر العقل وجوده وعدمه ـ يعني أنَّ الجائز العقلي في حَقِّهِ تعالى هو : ما أمكنا أي : فعل كلِّ ممكن وتركه ، لكنَّه عبر عن الفعل بقوله: إيجادا وعن التّرك بقوله : إعْدَامًا وَمَثَل لبعض جُزئيَّات الجائز : فعله بقوله: كَرَزْقِه بفتح الراء، من إضافة المصدر وتركه حقه في لافاعله ، أي : كرَزق الله العبدَ الغِنَى ضِدُّ الفقر ، مِثال للفعل ، ومثالُ التّرك: عدمُ رَزْقِ الله العبد إيَّاه . وإذا نظرتَ إلى هذا التقدير اندفع عندك ما يقال: إنه لا فائدة في الإخبار ؛ لأنَّ المبتدأ والخبر شيء واحد، لأنَّ الجائز هو الممكن، والممكن هو الجائز، فكأنه قال: والجائز جائز في حقه تعالى أو قال والممكن ممكن في حقه تعالى. و«ما» في قوله: ما أمكنا اسم موصول ، وهو دالّ على العموم، أي: أنه يجوز عليه تعالى إيجاد كل ممكن أو إعدامه ، وهذا الكلام يردُّ على طائفتين: الأولى: الذين يقولون بأنَّ بعض الأمور الممكنة واجبة عليه الله ، كالمعتزلة الذين ذهبوا إلى يجب عليه تعالى أن يفعل ما هو الصالح لعباده.. والطائفة الثانية: الذين ذهبوا إلى أن بعض الأمور المُمكنة مستحيلة عليه تعالى، كالبَرَاهِمَة الذين ذهبوا إلى أن بعثة الرسل مستحيلة، وسيأتي الكلام على كل واحدة من هاتين المسألتين تفصيلا . إن شاء الله تعالى.
Section of Divine Matters - and it is what is valid in the view of intellect its existence and non-existence - meaning that the intellectual permissible in His right, Exalted is He, is: 'what is possible for us' i.e., doing every possible and leaving it, but he expressed doing by his saying: 'bringing into existence' and leaving by his saying: 'annihilating.' And he gave an example of some particulars of the permissible: its doing by his saying: 'like His providing' with fatha on the ra, from the addition of the source to its agent, i.e., like God's providing the servant wealth, the opposite of poverty, an example of the act, and an example of leaving: God's not providing the servant it. And if you consider this estimation, there is repelled from you what is said: there is no benefit in the informing because the subject and predicate are one thing, because the permissible is the possible, and the possible is the permissible, so it is as if he said: 'and the permissible is permissible in His right' or he said 'and the possible is possible in His right.' And 'ma' in his saying 'ma amkanna' is a relative noun, and it indicates generality, i.e., that it is permissible for Him, Exalted, to bring into existence every possible or annihilate it. And this statement refutes two groups: the first: those who say that some possible matters are obligatory upon God, like the Mu'tazila who held that He, Exalted, must do what is most beneficial for His servants; and the second group: those who held that some possible matters are impossible upon Him, Exalted, like the Brahmins who held that the sending of messengers is impossible. And the discussion of each of these two issues will come in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a refutation. The author begins by defining the section on Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat) and then defines the intellectual permissible (al-ja'iz al-'aqli) in relation to Allah. He explains that it means 'what is possible for us' (ma amkanna), i.e., the doing or leaving of any possible thing. The author clarifies that the poet expressed 'doing' as 'bringing into existence' (ijadan) and 'leaving' as 'annihilating' (i'daman). He gives an example: God's providing wealth (the act) and not providing it (the leaving). Next, the author addresses a potential objection: that this definition is tautological because the permissible and the possible are the same. He shows that the phrase 'ma amkanna' (what is possible for us) is a relative noun indicating generality, so the meaning is that it is permissible for Allah to bring into existence or annihilate every possible thing, which is a substantive claim. He then states that this statement refutes two groups: (1) the Mu'tazila, who claim that some possible things are obligatory upon Allah, such as doing what is most beneficial for His servants; and (2) the Brahmins, who claim that some possible things are impossible for Allah, such as sending messengers. The author promises a detailed discussion of these two issues later.

Misconception: The definition of al-ja'iz as 'what is possible' is tautological and adds no information.
Correction: The relative noun 'ma' indicates generality, so the predicate clarifies that every possible thing is permissible for Allah to do or leave, refuting restrictive views.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is not obligated to do what is best for you, does that shake or strengthen your trust in His wisdom? Are you ready to accept whatever He wills, even when it contradicts your expectations?
?
The Barahima doubted that Allah could send messengers. Examine your own heart: do you ever limit what Allah can do in your life? Name one area where you act as if something is impossible for Him and commit to trusting His power.
?
The Mu'tazila's error stemmed from imposing human standards of goodness on Allah. Where in your life do you judge Allah's actions by your own sense of fairness? Will you surrender that judgment and affirm that He is free to do as He wills, knowing it is always just?

Promise and Threat: The Agreed Ruling
Sanusi defines al-wa'd and al-wa'id and states the agreement that promise cannot be broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of promise (al-wa'd) and threat (al-wa'id) in Islamic theology, and why promise is absolutely binding while threat is not, according to the agreement of Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Core Idea
Promise binds; threat may be broken—for breaking a promise entails deficiency, but breaking a threat does not.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies two crucial terms in Islamic theology: promise (al-wa'd) and threat (al-wa'id). He establishes that Allah's promise of reward to believers is absolutely certain and cannot fail, because breaking it would imply imperfection. However, threat of punishment is different: it may be forgiven. This distinction is agreed upon by both Ash'ari and Maturidi schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp what al-wa'd (promise) and al-wa'id (threat) mean in this context. Note that 'promise' refers to the promised thing, and 'fulfiller' means the giver.
The passage starts by explaining the linguistic meaning.
2
Examine the proof from Qur'an and logic
See how the author uses Qur'anic verses (Ar-Rum 6 and Aal Imran 9) to establish certainty, then rational argument: if promise failed, it would entail lying, foolishness, deficiency—all impossible for Allah.
The proof is both textual and rational.
3
Recognize the agreed ruling
Notice that this understanding—that promise cannot be broken—is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. This is not a point of disagreement.
4
Understand the distinction from threat
The commentator explains that threat (al-wa'id) is different: it is not impossible to break it, so Allah may forgive instead of punishing. This is because breaking a threat does not entail deficiency, unlike promise.
The distinction is crucial for understanding divine mercy and justice.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it upholds both Allah's perfect justice (promise binding) and His mercy (threat may be forgiven).
This balances attributes of justice and mercy.

مَسأَلةُ الوَعْدِ وَالوَعِيدِ وَبَيَانُ الخِلَاف فِيهَا
فهو مفعولُ (منجز)، والمنجز : المُعطي ، والمراد بالوعد الموعود به ، ومعنى الكلام: أنَّ وعد الله تعالى المؤمنين بالجنة والثواب لا يتخلَّفُ شرعاً ، وهذا أمر مقطوع به لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ الله لا يُخلِفُ اللهُ ﴾ [الروم: ٦] ، وقوله جل ذكره: إنَّ اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ ) [آل عمران: ٩] والمراد بالميعاد الوعد كما قاله المفسرون ، وبيان وجه كونه مقطوعا به : أنَّه لو تخلّف إعطاء الله تعالى الموعود به لزم الكذب والسَّفَهُ والخُلْف والنقص، وكل واحد من هذه الأشياء محال على الله تعالى ؛ فما أدى إليها أو إلى بعضها فهو محالٌ أيضا، وهذا المقدار متفق عليه بين كل من الأشاعرة والماتريدية.
قسم الإلهيات
الأزل (١)؛ إذ المراد لا يتخلَّفُ عن الإرادة ؛ لأنَّه لو تخلّف إعطاء الموعود به لزمَ الكِذبُ والسَّفه والخُلْف والتَّبديل في القول ، وهو خلاف قوله تعالى: إِنَّكَ لَا تُخلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ ) [آل عمران : ١٩٤) مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى [ف: ۲۹] ، فالتَّوابُ فضل من الله تعالى وَعَدَ به المُطِيعَ ، فيفى له به ؛ لأنَّ الخُلْف في الوعد نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه، بخلاف الوعيد؛ فإنَّه لا يستحيل إخلافه، فيجوز.
(1) قوله : (الذي سبقت به إرادته الأولى : وعده الذي وعد به على لسان نبيه أو في كتابه ، وإلا فالوعد والوعيد بالنظر للإرادة الأزلية لا يتخلفان ، وغرضنا التفرقة بينهما أفاده شيخنا، ولك أن تقول: هذا وصف كاشف إشارة إلى أنه يلزم الوعد الإرادة الأزلية ضرورة أنه لا يتخلف، والوعيد قد تسبق الإرادة بغفرانه فتدبر الأمير.
The question of promise and threat and the clarification of disagreement concerning it. So it is the object of (fulfiller), and fulfiller is the giver, and by promise is meant what is promised. And the meaning of the speech: that the promise of Allah the Exalted to the believers of Paradise and reward does not fail to occur legally, and this is a certain matter due to His saying the Exalted: {Promise of Allah, Allah does not fail His promise} [Ar-Rum: 6], and His saying the Majestic in remembrance: {Indeed Allah does not break the appointment} [Aal Imran: 9], and by appointment is meant promise as the exegetes said. And the clarification of the reason for it being certain: that if the giving of what is promised by Allah the Exalted were to fail, lying, foolishness, breaking of promise, and deficiency would follow, and each of these things is impossible for Allah the Exalted; so whatever leads to them or to some of them is also impossible. And this amount is agreed upon between both the Ash'aris and the Maturidis. Section of Ilahiyyat (Divine Matters). The eternal [note 1]; because the intended object does not fail to occur from the will; because if the giving of what is promised were to fail, lying, foolishness, breaking of promise, and alteration in speech would follow, and that opposes His saying the Exalted: {Indeed You do not break the appointment} [Aal Imran: 194] and {My word is not changed} [Qaf: 29]. So reward is a favor from Allah the Exalted which He promised to the obedient, He fulfills it for him; because breaking a promise is a deficiency which must be negated from Him the Exalted, unlike the threat; for indeed it is not impossible to break it, so it is permissible. (1) His saying: (which was preceded by His first will: His promise which He promised upon the tongue of His prophet or in His book, otherwise the promise and threat with respect to the eternal will do not fail, and our purpose is to distinguish between them—this was taught by our shaykh; and you may say: this is a descriptive indication that the promise necessitates the eternal will necessarily because it does not fail, and the threat may be preceded by the will of His forgiveness, so reflect, O prince.)
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines the two key terms wa'd (promise) and wa'id (threat) and establishes the agreed ruling on promise. The author begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning: the word 'fulfiller' (munjiz) means 'giver', and 'promise' refers to the thing promised. The doctrinal point is that Allah's promise of Paradise and reward to believers cannot fail to occur in the legal/religious sense. This is a certain matter proven by two Qur'anic verses: 'Promise of Allah, Allah does not fail His promise' and 'Indeed Allah does not break the appointment'—where 'appointment' means promise as the exegetes explain. The logical proof follows: if the giving of the promised reward failed to occur, it would entail lying, foolishness, breaking of promise, and deficiency—all of which are impossible for Allah. Hence the impossibility extends to anything that leads to them. The author notes that this much is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. Then follows a comment from the commentator (likely the author of Ithaaf) under the heading 'Section of Ilahiyyat'. He explains that the intended object (the promise) does not fail to occur from the eternal will; failing would contradict verses that deny change in Allah's word. Reward is a favor promised to the obedient, so He fulfills it because breaking a promise is a deficiency, but threat is different—breaking it is not impossible, so it is permissible. The footnote elaborates that promise and threat in relation to the eternal will never fail, but the purpose is to distinguish between them; the promise is necessarily linked to the eternal will, while the threat may be preceded by the will of forgiveness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Promise and threat are both equally binding on Allah.
Correction: Promise is absolutely binding because breaking it entails deficiency, but threat may be broken (forgiven) because breaking a threat does not entail deficiency.

Checkpoint
What is the agreed ruling on Allah's promise (al-wa'd) according to both Ash'aris and Maturidis?
A) It may be broken if Allah wills.
B) It cannot be broken because it would entail impossibility.
C) It is uncertain whether it will be fulfilled.
D) It is like a threat and may be broken.

Key Takeaway
The promise of Allah is absolutely binding because breaking it would imply imperfection, while the threat is not binding as it may be forgiven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that Allah's promise is absolutely binding because breaking it would be a deficiency, ask yourself: Do you live as if you truly trust His promise of Paradise? Where is your hope anchored—in your own deeds or in the certainty of His word? If your deeds falter, does your trust in His promise remain unshaken? Write down one specific situation this week where you will actively remind yourself of the binding promise to strengthen your hope.
?
Sanusi says breaking the threat is permissible—it does not involve deficiency. How does that affect the way you fear Allah? Does the possibility of forgiveness make you more hopeful or more careless? Examine your own heart: have you ever exploited Allah's mercy as a license to sin? Determine a concrete habit you will adopt to balance hope and fear correctly, knowing that the promise binds but the threat can be lifted.
?
The passage emphasizes that breaking a promise implies deficiency, which is impossible for Allah. Think of a promise you made to Allah or to another person that you broke or neglected. How does this contrast with the perfection of Allah's fulfillment? What does your broken promise reveal about yourself? Repent for it now, and make a firm intention to uphold one promise this week as an act of following Allah's attribute of truthfulness.

Definition of Kasb and Khalq in Ash'ari Theology
The author defines kasb and khalq with two alternative formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definitions of kasb (acquisition) and khalq (creation) and how they distinguish human and divine agency.

Core Idea
Kasb is human action without independent creation; khalq is God's exclusive creation.

This lesson introduces two key theological terms: kasb (acquisition) and khalq (creation). The author presents two definitions for each, clarifying the subtle difference between human and divine agency. Understanding these definitions is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on free will and responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage that defines kasb and khalq. Notice the two definitions given for each term.
2
Compare the definitions
Compare the definitions of kasb and khalq side by side. Identify the key phrase that distinguishes them: 'without the validity of independence' vs. 'with the validity of independence'.
3
Understand the consequence
Read the statement that 'kasb does not necessitate existence' but leads to the servant being charged. Reflect on why this distinction matters for moral responsibility.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Identify the key terms: kasb, khalq, maqdur, and temporal/eternal power. Write down their meanings.
These terms will recur in later lessons.
